# Aff -- Decolonial Fugitivity

⭐️Sophia, Iliyan, Sean, Sam, Soyam, Shubh, and Lena Legend ⭐️

# 1AC

## 1AC 

#### Arctic policy is [inextricably]{.underline} tied to [exclusionary]{.underline} identity politics that leverages [economic]{.underline} and [geopolitical]{.underline} domination to [subjugate]{.underline} the Indigenous "wilderness" of the Arctic under [settler colonial racial capitalism]{.underline}

**Gricius, 2022**

(Gabriella -- PhD Candidate in the Department of Political Science @
Colorado State University and a Graduate Fellow at the North American &
Arctic Defense and Security Network, has an MA in International Security
from the University of Groningen, "Pulling back the curtain:
coloniality-based narratives of wilderness in US Arctic policy," The
Polar Journal 12.2, 2022, pp.198-214)

[The [Arctic policy]{.mark} of the United States [is]{.mark} often
[analysed through a lens of]{.mark} geopolitical [competition]{.mark}.
Scholars tend to be [preoccupied with]{.mark}]{.underline} topics such
as perceived '[[icebreaker]{.mark} gap[s]{.mark}'
[and]{.mark}]{.underline} how American Arctic policy has addressed or
not addressed current [Arctic [security]{.mark}]{.underline}
realities.Footnote1 But how does the United States conceptualise its
relationship to the Arctic and what does that mean for American foreign
policy towards the region? Answering these questions is key to
understanding both where the United States is coming from and where it
is going as the Arctic continues to dominate headlines. In this article,
I argue that [[the U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [is incapable of
taking just any policy towards the Arctic]{.mark}. The [possibilities
are constrained]{.mark} because American foreign policy is moored
to]{.underline} some set of ideas about the 'wilderness', including the
Arctic. Indeed, I argue that in American foreign policy, ideas and
narratives on wilderness are [[based on coloniality]{.underline}]{.mark}
and are not only a common feature of American biographical continuity,
but they are also broadly consistent and somewhat predictable across
American Arctic policy through the Bush, Obama, and Trump
administrations. Indeed, coloniality-based narratives of wilderness --
including ideas of the wilderness as an economic resource, the
wilderness as diminishing and the wilderness as harsh and unwelcoming --
are visible across American Arctic policy from 2009 to 2021. Scholarly
literature has addressed how coloniality is consistent within Arctic
security policies, but it often takes a Canadian or Nordic lens rather
than an American one.Footnote2 In this article, I analyse American
Arctic policy using a framework drawn from coloniality and wilderness
literature and combining them with an ontological security approach that
highlights the selective activation and deactivation of narratives over
time to serve a broader autobiographical self. In doing so I demonstrate
how studies of coloniality can be combined with ontological security to
provide a more nuanced picture of how states deal with existential
anxiety and how much of themselves is based on coloniality. I begin by
outlining how the field of international relations has conceived of
wilderness in the past, specifically focusing on how [[wilderness has
been framed]{.mark} in the United States]{.underline}, which is grounded
[[in non-Indigenous, racist, and exclusionary identity
politics]{.underline}]{.mark}. I then discuss how concepts of settler
colonialism and coloniality complicate this framing of wilderness and
suggest what implications that has for Indigenous People. I propose that
ontological security studies (OSS) should take these concepts into
account when considering autobiographical state narratives while also
arguing that OSS adds to our understanding of how coloniality is
sustained through policy justifications today. I then use discourse
analysis to identify [narratives of coloniality and wilderness across US
[Arctic policies]{.mark}]{.underline} from the Bush, Obama, and Trump
administrations. These narratives have been separated into themes
including 1) the [[transform]{.underline}]{.mark}ation of [the
[wilderness into resources]{.mark}]{.underline}, 2) the wilderness as
diminishing, and 3) the wilderness as harsh and unwelcoming.
Additionally, there was some mythologising the Arctic and framing of the
region as a theatre for geopolitical competition from 2019 onwards. By
demonstrating the continuity of these narratives, I show that [while the
precise wording and framing may change slightly over time,
[coloniality-based narratives of wilderness are]{.mark} not only a
common feature, but also that they are [fundamental to]{.mark} American
[Arctic policy]{.mark}.]{.underline} My findings indicate that the
United States is constrained in how it creates Arctic policy; thus,
there are a limited number of ways that the Arctic as a wilderness is
conceptualised. That [these narratives]{.underline} have their basis in
coloniality also [demonstrate]{.underline}s [the continued colonial
influence underlying American Arctic policy.]{.underline} Narratives of
wilderness in the United States Wilderness has a complex history, both
in international relations as well as environmental studies more
broadly[. American [perceptions of wilderness]{.mark} have
[tend]{.mark}ed [towards]{.mark} two extremes, either 1) as pristine,
[untouched, and uninhabited, or]{.mark} 2) [something
exploitable]{.mark} for the benefit of extractive capitalist
practices.]{.underline} Over the past two centuries in the United
States, American society has given wilderness different meanings --
varying from deserted and savage, to a sublime way of getting closer to
God, to the American frontier invested with individualism and
masculinity, to a civilised leisure destination for upper middle-class
men, to now as a source of danger in the face of climate
change.Footnote3 Thus, for American society, wilderness has multiple and
changing definitions. Instead, it reflects broader hegemonic social
relations.Footnote4 These imaginaries have broad implications. For
example, [**imagining the [wilderness as]{.mark} pristine and [untouched
legitimates the expulsion of Indigenous People]{.mark}**. Imagining
[it]{.mark} as inherently separate from humans [allows humanity to evade
responsibility for caring for the
environment]{.mark}]{.underline}.Footnote5 [[Wilderness
myths]{.underline}]{.mark} have often been used to [create a sense of
national identity]{.underline}. However, this has often come [at the
expense of the lives of Indigenous People and]{.underline} was used to
[[erase Indigenous understandings of place]{.mark} and
landscape]{.underline}. For example, Turner's 1893 Frontier thesis
suggested that taming the wilderness was synonymous with developing an
independent American identity. However, Turner barely referred to
Indigenous People, which contributed to the erasure of them and their
history.Footnote6 Wilderness is also often thought of as socially
exclusionary, privileging only white, male, and middle class, and is
commodified -- produced for tourism, mining, conservation, and other
extractive uses.Footnote7 This type of thinking comes up again and again
in American society, whether in Theodore Roosevelt's The Wilderness
Hunter and his conservation policies as president, prominent American
conservationist Aldo Leopold's 'wilderness ethic', as well as in modern
environmentalist thought.Footnote8 The rise of the American conservation
movement is associated with many of these hierarchical and political
values that not only erase Indigenous People but at their core are
regarded as anti-Indigenous.Footnote9 While the rise of this movement is
often associated with urban wealthy elites seeking to preserve national
parks, preserving access to fish and game, and business environmentalism
-- other environmental -- and often marginalised -- voices exist too
from marginalised communities with different auspices including a deep
green ecology approach, giving agency to nature, alongside Indigenous
approaches to environmental protection. The urban-elite driven
conservation movement both reinforces settler-colonial relations and
relies on the myth of pure wilderness and the disavowal of original
settler violence.Footnote10 This is prefaced on the ontological
assumption that the wilderness is at its purest when there are no human
inhabitants. However, this is certainly not limited to the urban-elite
driven conservation movement but is also prevalent through how American
policy conceptualises the wilderness across the government and is
visible in the narratives the United States uses to conceptualise
itself. There are other ways of thinking about wilderness that are not
rooted in the dominant American narratives perpetuated by the state.
While there is no essentialised Indigenous way of thinking and knowing
land, there are some commonalities in how wilderness is conceptualised.
Many origin stories from Indigenous People involve intimate relations
with the earth and highlight and ethics of care, suggesting that land is
not only life giving but an active participant in the relationship
between humanity and nature.Footnote11 Despite this, Indigenous
environmental concerns in Alaska has often been belied by careless and
wanton use of resources in Alaskan Native history.Footnote12 Thus, in
contrast to American ways of separating humanity from wilderness and
nature, many Indigenous ways of knowing about wilderness combat that
binary.Footnote13 However, while it is important to acknowledge
Indigenous scholarship on wilderness, doing so runs the risk of
flattening the spiritual and place-based aspects of Indigenous knowledge
without acknowledging the contemporary realities lived by Indigenous
People. This, in turn, can act as a second colonialisation, this time of
knowledge.Footnote14 So, when thinking about how wilderness is conceived
of today, we must also consider ongoing dynamics of settler colonialism
and how they impact Indigenous People today. Wilderness, settler
colonialism, and ontological security When we consider wilderness
through the lens of settler colonialism, it is important to first
consider terminology. Scholars define settler colonialism in differing
but important ways. While Eichler and Baumeister (Citation2021) suggest
it is the commitment of settlers to establish themselves permanently in
the periphery,Footnote15 Coulthard (Citation2014) argues that [[the
set]{.mark}tler-[col]{.mark}onial [relationship is]{.mark} one
[characterised by domination,]{.mark}]{.underline} that [[this]{.mark}
relationship [exists to facilitate]{.mark} the [dispossession]{.mark} of
Indigenous People]{.underline}.Footnote16 Todd (Citation2017) proposes
that settler colonialism reproduces the ideologies, relationships, and
physicality of colonialism as ongoing.Footnote17 Other scholars note
that settler colonialism is also premised on the idea that land is
transformed into private property.Footnote18 What these definitions have
in common is that [there is some continued relationship between
coloniser, colonised, and land that uses naturalised social and
political hierarchies]{.underline}. This leads into thinking about
coloniality, which is key for the purpose of this article. Coloniality,
simply put, is what survives colonialism.Footnote19 According to
Maldonado-Torres (Citation2007, 243), 'Coloniality, instead, refers to
long-standing patterns of power that emerged as a result of colonialism
... it is maintained alive in books, in the criteria for academic
performance, in cultural patterns, in common sense, in the self-image of
peoples, in aspirations of self, and so many other aspects of our modern
experience.'Footnote20 If coloniality is what exists today that is
inherited from past colonial practices, then we can assume that these
hierarchies are present in relations between and within states, as well
as in policy, knowledge production, and across how states handle
creating policy in the Arctic. While the argument that coloniality is
alive and well today is thoroughly addressed in the post-colonial,
decolonial, and Indigenous literature, ontological security studies
(OSS) adds a new dimension to the discussion. OSS has not traditionally
addressed the continuation of colonial legacies as a key part of state
autobiographical narratives. However, what OSS highlights is how one of
the mechanisms through which coloniality is continued is through the
selective activation and deactivation of certain narratives. Rather than
focus on an actor's need for physical security, ontological security
brings attention to security as being. In other words, ontological
security refers to an actor's need for a secure identity that contains a
sense of biographical continuity and brings an emphasis to what stories
and narratives we tell ourselves about ourselves, the relationship we
have with others and the land we conquer, live on, or occupy. Adopting
this lens focuses attention on the relationship between anxiety and
identity, and especially how autobiographical narratives and routines
manage existential identity as a daily yet often unconscious process. In
short, it leads us to ask how do specific narrative anchor state
self-identity and implies that these narratives have an impact on
current stressors and problems.Footnote21 Thus, the scholarship suggests
that scholars should seek out routines, particular narratives and how
ideas are held constant over time to mitigate anxiety. Anxiety, here, is
the ordering principle of how states interact with the world and what
they must manage to feel secure. States can never solve anxiety, but
they can manage it through routines and narratives. OSS has been used to
study securitisation, moral injury, narration of state identity,
radicalisation, and many other questions of international
politics.Footnote22 OSS is a useful narrative for answering the question
of how the United States conceptualises the wilderness in its Arctic
policy. This is because the type of narrative that the United States
uses is indicative of how it manages existential anxiety and how that
selection of narratives is chosen is key to understanding what
underlying principle drives American Arctic policy. In other words, how
does the United States manage its all-encompassing existential anxiety
in the regional case of the Arctic and what narratives are at play?
Which narratives are chosen can tell us something about those underlying
anxieties? Wilderness narratives point to coloniality. Further, OSS
draws attention to biographical continuity -- an idea that this research
aims to flesh out by considering continuity in American Arctic policy
across different presidencies and administrations. OSS literature has
not often addressed the role of coloniality in the shaping of state
autobiographical narratives. While some scholars have noted that
established hierarchies, identities, and relationships play an important
role in colonial biographical continuity, this is far from the
norm.Footnote23 Instead, literature has looked at the role of colonial
subjugation -- namely that the emotional trauma from colonialisation has
influence on how states react to anxiety.Footnote24 Thus, bringing in
understandings of coloniality, particularly in how wilderness is
conceived of, in coloniser state identity is key for advancing OSS.
Importantly, OSS can also add to post-colonial, decolonial, and
Indigenous literature when considering the mechanisms through which
coloniality is sustained. OSS literature has discussed how states
selectively activate and deactivate parts of their autobiographical
narratives to fit new events and new anxieties into their
worldview.Footnote25 The instrumental use of these narratives adds
nuance to the discussion of how places like the United States may take
different stances to the Arctic over time, but that they all rely on
coloniality to reinforce power relations that place the United States on
top of social and political hierarchies. Bringing together
understandings of coloniality, settler colonialism, wilderness, and
ontological security is essential for the purposes of this article. This
is because I argue that in sustaining its ontological security, the
United States uses coloniality-based narratives of wilderness in the
broader context of settler colonialism. In other words, the mechanisms
through which the United States supports its Arctic policy reveal
coloniality-based narratives of wilderness, narratives which have been
selectively activated throughout different administrations (Bush, Obama,
and Trump), suggesting that the United States' ontological security
relies on such framings. Methodology The main question underpinning my
research is: what types of narratives of wilderness are present in
American Arctic policy and are they present consistently across
different presidencies? To answer this question, I conducted a discourse
analysis of 15 significant American Arctic policy documents from 2009 to
2021 -- covering the Bush, Obama, and Trump presidencies. As of the
writing of this article, there are no documents that have been
officially produced by the Biden administration and thus the analysis
only covers through the end of the Trump administration. Before
preceding it is important to note that of course American Arctic policy
has a much longer history than the Bush, Obama, and Trump
administrations and limiting my analysis to these three administrations
has the possibility to distort the context of policy. Taking this
shortened time analysis allows for a more detailed content analysis of
fewer documents -- drawing out textual references to coloniality in
American ontological security. Moreover, scholars have suggested that
beginning with the Bush administration's NSPD-66 document is a good
entry point for any analysis of American Arctic policy as it was driven
by the impetus of the 2007 Russian flag planting the proactive
restatement of American interest in the region.Footnote26 Further, this
research is focused primarily on coloniality rather than great power
competition, either with Russia or the Soviet Union or climate change.
Certainly, these two variables are important to consider in Arctic
politics and in this American Arctic policy analysis -- and will best be
served in future research. I began by looking for how wilderness was
conceptualised in these documents, exploring how context shaped the
wilderness and the Arctic environment in particular ways. I also looked
for examples of coloniality in the documents connected to the
wilderness. I then conducted a more quantitative-based analysis, looking
for both the presence as well as the absence of specific words including
'diminish, 'harsh', 'wilderness', 'frontier', 'pristine', and
'threatened'. By gathering information both on the broader context of
how the wilderness was described and collecting quantitative-based
information on those framings, I aimed to triangulate if the United
States was using coloniality-based narratives of the wilderness to
justify its policy over a series of American presidential
administrations. The next sections will describe a variety of themes
that were found by using this discourse analysis method including: 1)
the wilderness as economic opportunity, 2) the wilderness as diminishing
and 3) the wilderness as harsh and unwelcoming and additional
mythologising and geopolitical narratives. US Arctic policy: wilderness
as economic opportunity 'The Arctic region's energy resources factor
into a core component of our national security strategy: energy
security. The region holds sizeable proved and potential oil and natural
gas resources that will likely continue to provide valuable supplies to
meet U.S. energy needs' 2013 National Strategy [Across the broad range
of Arctic policy documents published by the United States]{.underline},
perhaps [[the most common narrative that surrounds the Arctic is]{.mark}
the wilderness's [capacity for economic
opportunity]{.mark}.]{.underline} Starting with [[the]{.mark} 2009 [Navy
Arctic Roadmap]{.mark}]{.underline}, many American policies [[highlight
the]{.mark} Arctic's [potential for]{.mark} resource
[extraction]{.mark}.]{.underline} Among the roadmap's goals, [the policy
calls for an assessment]{.underline}, (p. 24) ' ... with particular
emphasis on the time frame in which ice extent and thickness will allow
[for trans-Arctic shipping and significant increase in]{.underline}
intra-Arctic shipping resource [extraction]{.underline}.' Gathering
information about when such extraction and economic opportunities will
be available is a key to these policies. As the quote at the beginning
of this section illustrates, the Arctic's resources are even framed as a
benefit for the energy security of the United States. As I illustrate
below, [such [framing]{.mark}s of [the Arctic as this realm for
extractive purposes]{.mark}]{.underline} and economic prosperity [are
not only common]{.underline}, but [they [are]{.mark}]{.underline} also
[[the norm in]{.mark} how American Arctic [policy]{.mark} describes the
region.]{.underline} Throughout the many policies released by the
different branches of the defence sector of the government from 2019 to
2021, the region is framed continuously as one with many resources that
can be exploited. The Coast Guard's Strategic Outlook begins their
entire policy by listing the economic value of different extractive
practices on the third page of the document, describing the region as
(p. 13) ' ... likely to be home to a wealth of undiscovered resources,
which will become more accessible as environmental conditions change.'
In a key speech Pompeo gave to the Arctic Council in 2019, he describes
the Arctic ' ... at the forefront of opportunity and abundance.' He goes
on to even qualify what he means by opportunity, stating 'It houses 13%
of the world's undiscovered oil, 30% of its undiscovered gas, and an
abundance of uranium, rare earth minerals, gold, diamonds, and millions
of square miles of untapped resources. Fisheries galore.' American
documents and framings of the Arctic in this way seem to be consistent
over time, with the 2020 Air Force Arctic Strategy, repeating the same
listing of economic opportunities that are to be gained from Arctic
engagement, (p. 7) 'The Arctic is estimated to harbour over 90 billion
barrels of oil reserves, 30% of the world's untapped natural gas, and
around one trillion dollars' worth of rare earth minerals.' In the 2021
Army Arctic Strategy, similar words are used to discuss the Arctic, as
(p. 3) ' ... opening up new opportunities for natural resource
extraction, shipping routes, and commercial fishing' and as (p. 4)
'decreased sea ice and glacial mass will open access to currently
unclaimed natural resources.' Most recently, the Department of Homeland
Security's 2021 Arctic Strategy frames climate change as key to (p. 13)
'unlock\[ing\] previously inaccessible resources and trade route
opportunities for both Arctic and non-Arctic states.' Moreover, this
language of economic opportunity is present in early documents as well.
As referenced above, as early as the 2009 Navy Roadmap and through the
Obama administration, the region was framed as a new boon for developing
energy resources, and a new place to develop energy security by
exploiting oil and gas resources. [This type of narrative shows
continuity and consistency over time -- suggesting that [the
U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [is incapable of conceiving its policies
any differently]{.mark} when it is so fundamentally moored in ideas of
economic extraction.]{.underline} One might expect that treating the
wilderness as this economic boon would result in the usage of words like
'frontier' and 'wilderness' to frame the region as uninhabited and one
ripe for exploitation and exploration. However, both words were only
used twice in the 2013 National Strategy for the Arctic, once in the
2013 Managing for the Future in a Rapidly Changing Arctic policy, and
once in the 2019 Department of Defence Arctic Strategy. The word
'frontier', like 'wilderness', also has a history and it is likely that
while its usage may have been more reasonable in 2013, as time has
passed, the narrative of the Arctic as a frontier became less accepted
and thus using more economic language and examples to describe
commodification were used instead. Much of this framing comes back to
the wilderness as commodifiedFootnote27 -- produced for extractive
purposes and suggesting that the wilderness here as framed as a resource
that will be used for the benefit of the American economy. [This
instrumentalized extractive framing of the wilderness also comes back to
coloniality -- more crucially that the desire to tame wilderness and
transform that land into usable resources is of colonial
origin.]{.underline}Footnote28

#### Ongoing colonialism in the arctic causes inter-generational harm that is both material and psychosocial 

**Malik and Ford, 2025**

(Ishfaq Hussain Malik -- PhD in Geography, Aligarh Muslim University,
James D. Ford -- Professor and Priestley Chair in Climate Adaptation @
University of Leeds, "Barriers and limits to adaptation in the Arctic,"
Current Opinion in Environmental Sustainability, April 2025,
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120)

The [loss of traditional knowledge is a]{.underline}n important barrier
to adaptation in the Arctic. External pressures such as globalisation
cause [resource [extraction, displacement, and environmental
degradation, impact]{.mark}]{.underline}ing [[Indigenous knowledge
systems,]{.mark} cultural [practices, languages, and
institutions]{.mark}]{.underline} \[21\], hence [[perpetuating epistemic
injustice]{.mark}.]{.underline} Economic and environmental changes
exacerbate this, [[causing generational discontinuities in the transfer
of]{.mark} traditional [knowledge]{.mark}]{.underline} to younger
generations \[81\]. There is limited prioritisation of Indigenous
Peoples in adaptation policy development and support to respond to
climate impacts 10, 13. [[The lack of engagement and exclusion of
Indigenous Peoples]{.mark}, knowledge, and perspectives [from
policy]{.mark}]{.underline} formulations [is a barrier that [affects the
implementation of]{.mark} effective culturally sensitive adaptation
strategies and [policies]{.mark} tailored and relevant to Indigenous
Peoples]{.underline} 49, 87. [[Cultural practices]{.mark} such as
sharing networks, communal hunting, oral traditions, storytelling, and
connections to the land and ocean [are affected by loss of traditional
hunting grounds and wildlife due to climate change]{.mark}]{.underline}
impacts \[29\], [[affecting food security and weakening community
agency]{.mark}, cultural identity, resilience, and adaptive
capacity]{.underline}. [Indigenous knowledge systems are]{.underline}
important for adaptation and are [at risk due to]{.underline} cultural
[assimilation]{.underline}, modernisation, generational gaps, [and
transformative environmental changes]{.underline} 108, 77. This loss
creates barriers, as it reduces adaptive capacity and coping mechanisms,
undermining the ability of communities to respond effectively to
changing climatic conditions. It reduces sustainable resource management
and adaptive capacities and practices where communities could
potentially lose critical insights that inform locally appropriate and
culturally sensitive adaptation strategies 23, 87. [In Scandinavia, Sami
herding traditions, which involve adaptive rotational grazing to
maintain reindeer populations, are often disregarded]{.underline} in
conservation planning. [Instead, policymakers prioritise
E]{.underline}uropean [U]{.underline}nion environmental [regulations
that enforce fixed grazing]{.underline} boundaries 100, 68. [In Canadian
Arctic, younger generations are increasingly disconnected from
traditional hunting practices due]{.underline} to the influence of
modern lifestyles, climate change, and the decline in wildlife
populations 56, 81. This loss of traditional knowledge creates a barrier
to adaptation, as communities lose the ability to respond effectively to
changing environmental conditions. The role of gendered labour norms
acts as a barrier and plays a significant role in shaping adaptive
capacity and decision-making. While men traditionally occupied roles as
hunters, herders, and fishermen, climate change is altering these
economic foundations, necessitating new employment opportunities \[7\].
In Sami communities, men's ability to continue reindeer herding is being
undermined by shrinking pastures, forcing women into new economic roles
\[73\]. In many Arctic communities, women are leaving for education and
employment opportunities, which can both mitigate and exacerbate
vulnerabilities \[12\]. While improved education and job access can
enhance resilience, they may also lead to the outmigration of skilled
individuals, further straining community cohesion and adaptive capacity.
In Greenland, many Indigenous women are leaving their communities to
seek education and employment in Denmark and urban areas, which provides
economic empowerment but also weakens intergenerational knowledge
transfer and community cohesion 54, 82. While these individual choices
offer economic mobility, they weaken the transmission of traditional
adaptation strategies, presenting both a barrier and a limit --- a limit
because the cultural shift away from subsistence practices is often
irreversible. Social norms tied to [traditional [subsistence practices
are being]{.mark} fundamentally [disrupted]{.mark} by climate
change]{.underline}, leading to limits on adaptation. [An important
example of this limit is the loss of key wildlife species essential to
Indigenous diets and cultural identity]{.underline}. The decline of
[[caribou]{.underline}]{.mark} [[populations]{.underline}]{.mark} [in
the]{.underline} Canadian [Arctic has [reached a point where]{.mark}
some [communities can no longer rely on this culturally and
nutritionally important species]{.mark}]{.underline} 8, 93. This
represents a limit to adaptation, as the loss of caribou undermines food
security, cultural identity, and traditional practices, significantly
impacting Inuit communities. [Conservation efforts, such as restricting
caribou hunting]{.underline}, address ecological sustainability but
[force communities to abandon traditional food-sharing
networks]{.underline}, important for survival and social cohesion
\[42\]. In Greenland, Inuit hunters rely on sea ice for seal and whale
hunting, which is economically and culturally significant. However, with
sea ice loss accelerating, these traditional hunting grounds are
disappearing \[37\]. This represents a limit --- without ice, these
practices cannot continue. This leads to both food insecurity and
cultural erosion. Individual values Arctic Indigenous communities are
not homogenous; individual values and economic preferences vary
significantly across regions, influenced by local economic
opportunities, cultural activities, state policies, and
place/community-specific histories. While some communities remain deeply
connected to subsistence economies --- such as Nenets reindeer herders
in Siberia or Iñupiaq whalers in Alaska --- others, particularly younger
generations, are transitioning towards wage-based employment, education,
and urban employment 26, 27. Sami herders are increasingly using modern
economic practices, such as using drones, GPS tracking, Geographic
Information System, and market-based reindeer farming, helping them
adapt to a changing climate 35, 41. In contrast, Siberian reindeer
herders often lack access to such technologies due to economic
disparities and centralised Russian governance, making adaptation
difficult \[48\]. In Alaska, Indigenous women often face limited access
to health, education, and employment opportunities, affecting community
resilience 60, 86. This barrier is compounded by the outmigration of
skilled individuals, further straining community cohesion \[60\]. This
migration trend, while individually beneficial, weakens the adaptive
capacity of communities, as fewer young people remain to sustain
traditional hunting, fishing, and herding practices. As climate change
alters the Arctic environment, many Indigenous communities are forced to
shift away from traditional subsistence practices, leading to increased
dependence on expensive technologies for food gathering, imported foods,
and fuel \[76\]. The reliance on technologies such as high-efficiency
snowmobiles and gasoline for transportation, advanced fishing equipment
and boats for food gathering and travelling, and GPS systems and
satellite phones for navigation has increased 20••, 23, 84. However,
buying these technologies is costly, imposing a significant social and
financial strain on communities and creating social inequities. This is
exacerbated by an increased reliance on expensive store-bought foods, a
high cost of living, and dependence on global markets and price
fluctuations \[••65\]. This economic shift presents a barrier for
lower-income households that cannot afford adaptation costs, as well as
a limit for communities that have surpassed economic feasibility
thresholds. Inability to adapt due to financial constraints presents a
significant limit to adaptation. In Chukotka, Russia, many Indigenous
communities live in poverty, with limited access to resources and
infrastructure \[53\]. This financial constraint creates a limit to
adaptation, as communities cannot afford the technologies or
infrastructure needed to cope with climate change. The limited mobility
due to fixed settlements acts as a limit. In Alaska, many Indigenous
communities were forcibly relocated to fixed settlements. These
settlements are now vulnerable to rising sea levels and permafrost thaw,
limiting the ability of communities to adapt through traditional
mobility practices 25, 51. Psychosocial factors Psychosocial factors
such as [cultural [alienation, mental health struggles,
intergenerational disconnect, and loss of trust]{.mark} in institutions
[shape]{.mark} how [Arctic Indigenous communities]{.mark} perceive,
engage with, and respond to climate change]{.underline}. [Indigenous
communities face historical trauma of forced displacement]{.underline}
and cultural alienation \[88\]. Many Indigenous communities are facing
the threat of climate-induced relocation, as coastal erosion, permafrost
thawing, and extreme weather events threaten settlements that have
existed for generations 12, 51. However, [relocation is not just a
physical challenge --- it represents a rupture in cultural continuity,
identity, and psychological well-being]{.underline}. In Alaska, the
communities of Shishmaref, Newtok, and Kivalina, are facing forced
relocation due to rising sea levels and coastal erosion 25, 9. For many
community members, this [[displacement]{.underline}]{.mark} represents
more than just losing a home --- it [[means losing]{.mark} a way of
[life, severing]{.mark}]{.underline} their [[spiritual and cultural
ties]{.mark}]{.underline} to the land \[71\]. In Siberia, Indigenous
Evenki and Nenets herders face disruptions to traditional migration
routes due to industrial expansion and resource extraction \[96\]. These
changes force some herders into fixed settlements, where they experience
high levels of psychological distress and increased dependency on state
welfare systems \[52\]. This psychological trauma represents an
adaptation limit --- once the connection to land is severed, a
fundamental part of Indigenous identity is lost.

#### Violence against [Indigenous life]{.underline} and [agency]{.underline} outweighs any other impact

or systematic restrictions upon a group's ability to influence
government policy regarding designation and defence against security
threats to that group. Securitization nondominance reflects both
structural and institutional power, because it is both direct and
constitutive of actors, and diffused through regulation of the
interactions between particular social actors. Securitization
non-dominance is thus a reflection of broader forms of nondominance as
they pertain to the ability to construct and respond to in/security. The
argument of securitization non-dominance is fully developed in Chapter
3, and the forms of non-dominance experienced by Arctic Indigenous
peoples are discussed further below and in their specific national
contexts in Chapters 4-7. 2.2 Indigenous Peoples in the Arctic
Indigenous peoples in the circumpolar region exhibit both typical and
atypical traits for indigenous peoples in contemporary global politics.
On the one hand, they are historically colonized populations who no
longer exert full decision-making authority over their traditional
lands. They also demonstrate clear continuity with pre-colonial
practices and social organization, due in part to the relatively recent
experiences of colonization by many Arctic Indigenous peoples,
especially in North America. On the other hand, most of the Arctic
states are stronger democratic systems with rule of law, economic and
social benefits, and respect for indigenous rights than many other
countries with indigenous populations. As discussed below, Arctic
Indigenous peoples enjoy among the highest qualities of life and
greatest degrees of 113 Gunhild Hoogensen and Kirsti Stuvøy, "Gender,
Resistance, and Human Security," Security Dialogue 37, no. (2006): 219.
42 political autonomy and inclusion of any indigenous peoples in the
world. With the notable exception of Russia, Arctic Indigenous peoples
have politically organized themselves into formations that sometimes
challenge the preferences of their colonial state governments without
fear of overt political repression, violence, or retaliation.
[[They]{.mark} thus enjoy a **higher degree** of **political freedom**
than most, while also [experiencing]{.mark} **[material]{.mark}**
conditions and **political non[dominance]{.mark}** [that]{.mark}
**[curtail]{.mark} their [political agency]{.mark}**,
[reflecting]{.mark} their **[status]{.mark} [within]{.mark}** their
**[settler-colonial]{.mark}** political [contexts]{.mark}. While
**[debates over indigeneity]{.mark}** and identity remain
[relevant]{.mark} in the circumpolar region, [by virtue]{.mark} of their
distinct structures of political representation **[Arctic Indigenous
peoples]{.mark}** are somewhat more clearly identifiable than
elsewhere.]{.underline} By definition, they comprise the Indigenous
peoples living in the Arctic regions of the eight circumpolar states.
Informed by the Cobo definition, the Arctic Human Development Report
defines Indigenous peoples as: Those peoples who were marginalized when
the modern states were created and identify themselves as indigenous
peoples. They are associated with specific territories to which they
trace their histories. They exhibit one or more of the following
characteristics: they speak a language that is different from that of
the dominant group(s); they are being [**[discriminated
against]{.mark}** [in]{.mark} the **[political]{.mark}**

**[legal]{.mark} [system]{.mark}**]{.underline}; their [**[cultures

as **[different]{.mark}** [from]{.mark} the **[rest]{.mark} [of]{.mark}
the [population]{.mark}**]{.underline}.114 Indigenous peoples number
approximately 500,000 out of the total 4 million inhabitants of the
circumpolar region, and, as shown in Figure 2, form a ring of
overlapping trans-state populations surrounding the Arctic Ocean. A
majority of these live in Russia, which also has the greatest diversity
of Indigenous peoples with over 41 different groups, including the more
populous Chukchi, Evenki, Khaka, Khanty, Nenet, Tuvan, and Yakut
peoples. Aleuts are indigenous people in the easternmost Russian region
of Kamchatka who also inhabit the islands off western Alaska. Mainland
Alaska is home to Athabaskan, Gwich'in, and Inuit (Alaskan Eskimo)
peoples whose territories cross into northern Canada. Inuit form a

Beothuk Haudenosaunee Haudenosaunee 44 and a plurality of the total
population in the Canadian Arctic, as well as a large majority of nearly
90 percent in neighbouring Greenland. Sámi are the only recognized
Indigenous people in all of Europe, comprising the entire indigenous
populations of Norway, Sweden, and Finland, with a small number also on
the Kola Peninsula in western Russia.115 While now rooted in the social
and political structures of particular states, the sociological
boundaries of Arctic Indigenous peoples are not consistent with the
colonial boundaries that have been imposed upon them. Indigenous peoples
serve as living reminders of pre-modern patterns of habitation in the
circumpolar region, and reflect relationships and associations that
transcend sovereign borders. The formal political involvement of
Indigenous peoples in the circumpolar region is one of the key features
of the post-Cold War Arctic order. In addition to significant degrees of
selfgovernment, political autonomy, or elected representation exercised
by Indigenous peoples throughout much of the region -- particularly Self
Rule for the Inuit majority in Greenland; selfgovernment or high
representation in public governments for Canadian Inuit through the
establishment of four land claim areas; and the separate Sámi
Parliaments of Norway, Sweden, and Finland -- Indigenous peoples have
also been central to the establishment and activities of the Arctic
Council. Founded in 1996 to provide a dedicated regional forum for
dialogue and cooperation, the Council owes much of the impetus for its
creation to the organization and activism of Indigenous advocacy groups.
Since the 1960s, Indigenous peoples across the Arctic increasingly
established representative organizations to both lobby governments and
speak on behalf of their people, who at the time very much remained
colonial subjects of their southernbased governments. John English,
historian of the Arctic Council, credits the efforts of these
organizations and the leaders they produced with the eventual success of
the Arctic Council negotiations: "Voices that were silenced in the
fifties became audible in the sixties, eloquent in the seventies, and
powerful and influential later. The colonized now came to the colonial
capitals no longer as subjects but as actors shaping their times and the
lives of their people."116 The emergence of Indigenous voices as a
potent political force would substantially alter the shape of the
circumpolar region. 115 Antoine Dubreuil, "The Arctic of the Regions:
Between Indigenous Peoples and Sub-National Entities -- Which
Perspective?" International Journal 66, no. 4 (2011): 923-938. 116 John
English, Ice and Water: Politics, Peoples, and the Arctic Council
(Toronto: Penguin, 2013), 95. 45 Arising from social movements for
Indigenous rights and representation, six groups were formally
incorporated into regional **[[governance]{.underline}]{.mark}** through
their inclusion as Permanent Participants on the Arctic Council: the
Arctic Athabaskan Council, Aleut International Association, Gwich'in
Council International, Inuit Circumpolar Council, Russian Association of
Indigenous Peoples of the North, and the Saami Council. As the first
international intergovernmental body in the world to grant indigenous
peoples formal status with rights to membership and participation
approximating that of the Member States, the Council provides Indigenous
peoples with representation at the premier forum for regional
cooperation. English argues that "it is a mark of the historic change
that the growing prominence of the Arctic Council with its indigenous
participants means that many rules will now be set not only in imperial
southern capitals but often by northern peoples."117 English's detailed
analysis of the Council's origins concurs with the conclusions of other
leading scholars like Oran Young that it is partly through the efforts
of Indigenous peoples that the Arctic has developed from a "region of
peripheries" into a coherent region of its own,118 and with Timo
Koivurova and Leena Heinämäki that Arctic Indigenous peoples have become
so key to regional politics as to enjoy a status close to that of
vetoholders.119 Although they continue to reflect the significant
differences of opportunity afforded by their respective historical
experiences and contemporary circumstances, Arctic Indigenous peoples
have been at the forefront of advancing the political interests of
indigenous peoples and their inclusion in organizations and fora
previously reserved for states. In this respect, Arctic Indigenous
peoples reflect the global achievements of indigenous peoples in
fostering an awareness of indigeneity as a basis for rights and
political inclusion, and establishing a social movement to support the
political project of "global indigenism."120 The adoption of the United
Nations Declaration of the Rights of Indigenous Peoples in 2007, the
creation of the UN Permanent Forum on Indigenous Issues, and moves
towards recognition and reparations for colonially inflicted wrongs all
signify the contemporary emergence of indigenous 117 English 2013, 298.
118 Young 2005, 9-10. 119 Koivurova and Heinämäki 2006, 104. 120 J.

121-131; Niezen 2003. 46 peoples as political actors. The [terms
[of]{.mark} this inclusion **remain constrained**, however, by the

Despite the significant progress that has been made by settler-colonial
governments and legal authorities in terms of acknowledging and
respecting indigenous rights and title, the relationships between
indigenous peoples and their governments remain structured by the
dominance of settler-colonial values, institutions, and interests.
Though more empowered than ever before, and more so than most indigenous
peoples elsewhere in the world, even in the Arctic, Indigenous peoples
lack the power to control their own collective futures. 2.3 Research,
Non-Dominance and Indigenous Peoples The non-dominance of Arctic
Indigenous peoples is reflected in two distinct forms: material and
non-material (ideational). The material reflections of political
non-dominance vary by country and are discussed in subsequent chapters,
but generally [include **[lower qualities]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}
[life]{.mark}, forms of **[governmental control]{.mark}**, and
**[restrictions]{.mark}** [upon]{.mark} [indigenous]{.mark} rights and
[autonomy]{.mark} [resulting]{.mark} [from]{.mark} "the
**[demoralising]{.mark} effects** of **[dispossession]{.mark}**, [forced
removals]{.mark}, **[open racism]{.mark}** and
**discrimination**]{.underline}, and [**[destruction]{.mark}**
[of]{.mark} language, **[identity]{.mark}**, [and]{.mark}
**[culture]{.mark}**]{.underline}."122 Less overt but more pernicious,
however, is [the **ideational** non-dominance that has resulted in the
**[discrediting]{.mark}** [of]{.mark} **[indigenous]{.mark}** forms of
knowledge and **[knowledge production]{.mark}**, the
[privileging]{.mark} [of]{.mark} [settler-colonial]{.mark} legal and

[of]{.mark} [indigenous]{.mark}** [ways of]{.mark} **knowing** and
[being]{.mark} **[relative]{.mark}** [to]{.mark} those of
**[settlers]{.mark}**. Ideational non-dominance is a form of power
[that]{.mark} [operates]{.mark} "[in]{.mark} [underlying]{.mark}
[social]{.mark} structures and [systems]{.mark} [of]{.mark}
[knowledge]{.mark} that advantage some and disadvantage
others]{.underline}."123 ."123 [Indigenous **[traditional
knowledge]{.mark}** and traditional ecological knowledge have long been
[viewed]{.mark} as **[threatening]{.mark}** [to]{.mark} the **[colonial
order]{.mark}**]{.underline}, and have been subjected to concerted
efforts at destruction, 121 Maaka and Fleras 2005, 12. 122 Maaka and
Fleras 2005, 26. 123 Barnett and Duvall 2005, 42. 47 erasure, and
forgetting. As Linda Tuhiwai Smith asserts, "the [**[negation]{.mark}**

part of **asserting** **colonial [ideology]{.mark}**]{.underline},
partly [because such views]{.underline} were regarded as clearly
'primitive' and 'incorrect' and mostly because they [challenged and
**resisted the vision** of colonization]{.underline}."124 [Colonial
[authorities]{.mark} sought to [render]{.mark} [indigenous populations
legible to]{.mark} **new forms** of **[state
power]{.mark}**]{.underline}, which necessitated [[structuring the
social terrain]{.mark}]{.underline} on which indigenous/non-indigenous
relations would occur [[in a manner **advantageous** to the
colonizer]{.underline}]{.mark}. This included depriving prior indigenous
knowledge and forms of organization of the legitimacy to challenge
settler-colonial organization and Western forms of knowledge by
subjecting them to alien standards of colonial law and morality,
preventing their transmission to younger generations, and eventually
co-opting them into certain settler-colonial decision-making processes
and academic research.125 [The result has been the **widespread
[discrediting]{.mark}** [of]{.mark} the scientific validity,
**[legal]{.mark} [weight]{.mark}**, [and]{.mark} [moral]{.mark}
[worth]{.mark} [of]{.mark} [indigenous]{.mark} [knowledge]{.mark}

from **[settler-colonial]{.mark}**/scientific **[rationalist]{.mark}
modes** of **[thought]{.mark}** and **action**.]{.underline}126 The
ideational dimension of indigenous non-dominance is important to specify
because it sets the context for the material dimension that is more
readily visible in indigenous/non-indigenous relations. The two are
intimately linked, as it is the severing of indigenous peoples' ties to
their cultural and epistemological heritage that facilitates the
material dispossession of their lands, rights, and resources, and
establishes the context for settler domination in their new societies.
Erasure occurs in two ways: through the exclusion and enforced
forgetting of traditional indigenous knowledge, and through the
displacement and disruption of societies guided by and responsible for
generating that knowledge. Vandana Shiva observes: "Over and above
rendering local knowledge invisible by declaring it non-existent or
illegitimate, the dominant system also makes alternatives disappear by
erasing or destroying the reality which they attempt to represent."127
The current Arctic context exemplifies this dynamic, wherein traditional
knowledge has been denied scientific authority and excluded from
official decision making at the 124 Linda Tuhiwai Smith, Decolonizing
Methodologies: Research and Indigenous Peoples (London: Zed, 1999), 29.
125 Deborah McGregor, "Coming Full Circle: Indigenous Knowledge,
Environment and Our Future," American Indian Quarterly 28, no. 3/4
(2004): 385-410; Tuhiwai Smith 1999. 126 Claude Denis, We Are Not You:
First Nations and Canadian Modernity (Peterborough: Broadview Press,
1997). 127 Quoted in Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 100. 48 same time that
settler-colonial political, economic, and social policies have wrought
changes on Arctic societies and ecosystems that undermine the
applicability of traditional knowledge in the first place. Relevant
examples range from human impacts on the Arctic ecosystem such that
traditional ecological knowledge no longer reflects the characteristics
of the circumpolar climate, to settler-colonial policies that have
discriminated against indigenous women with impacts on traditional
structures of community authority.128 The [**[rejection]{.mark}** and
delegitimization of indigenous knowledge]{.underline} [[provides]{.mark}
a **[vital precondition]{.mark}** [for]{.mark} the **[material]{.mark}**
[non]{.mark}-[dominance]{.mark} [experienced by **indigenous
peoples**]{.mark}]{.underline}, particularly as it pertains to the
inability to make decisions regarding traditional land-use on ancestral
territories, and related decisions over how indigenous peoples can and
should live in the modern world. 2.3.1 Decolonization and Indigenist
Research A central component of this dissertation involves the
understandings of Indigenous peoples towards the meaning of in/security
in the Arctic region. Given the importance of epistemology and knowledge
production to power relations between indigenous and non-indigenous
peoples, pursuing appropriate and respectful research involving
indigenous peoples is complicated by a history of Western academic
research fraught with deceit, injustice, abuse, appropriation, and
perpetuation of the very structures that render indigenous peoples
non-dominant within their national communities. One of the key functions
that academic research has performed in this process is the explicit or
implicit privileging of settler-colonial and state perspectives,
resulting in the erasure and denial of indigenous histories,
epistemologies, and contemporary interests.129 Far from just the
objective interpretation of facts through theory, Tuhiwai Smith notes
the "problem is that academic writing is a form of selecting, arranging
and presenting knowledge. It 128 The differences between Western
scientific and indigenous observations of Arctic climate change are
shown in the film Qapirangajuq: Inuit Knowledge and Climate Change,
which highlights some of the disagreements and differing interpretations
between indigenous and scientific understandings of the changing
environment. The film is available at
http://www.isuma.tv/inuit-knowledge-and-climate-change. For a discussion
of traditional knowledge and traditional forms of community authority
see Stephanie Irlbacher-Fox, Jackie Price, and Elena Wilson-Rowe,
"Women's Participation in Decision-Making: Human Security in the
Canadian Arctic," in Hoogensen Gjørv et al., Environmental and Human
Security in the Arctic (New York: Routledge, 2014). 129 Marie Battiste,
ed, Reclaiming Indigenous Voice and Vision (Vancouver: UBC Press, 2000);
Tuhiwai Smith 1999. 49 privileges sets of texts, views about the history
of an idea, what issues count as significant; and, by engaging in the
same process uncritically, we too can render indigenous writers
invisible or unimportant while reinforcing the validity of other
writers."130 If research into issues pertaining to indigenous peoples is
undertaken without consideration of their views, including but not
limited to the inclusion of indigenous voices and perspectives, then
that research contributes to the "cognitive imperialism" that
underpinned colonization.131 Perhaps especially for disciplines such as
political science, international relations, and security studies that
have primarily focused on state behaviour, the international system of
sovereign states, and the mechanisms of interstate violence and
cooperation that constitute global order, there has been little account
of indigenous peoples: "On the international scene it is extremely rare
and unusual when indigenous accounts are accepted and acknowledged as
valid interpretations of what has taken place."132 Therefore,
contemporary research must be conscious of its role in supporting or
silencing the voices of indigenous peoples and other non-dominant
groups, and the ethical implications of writing them out of academic
accounts of modern politics. To this end, Tuhiwai Smith promotes an
'indigenist' research agenda to help decolonize the methodological and
epistemological approaches of academic research. For indigenous
scholars, decolonizing research may be integral to their individual and
collective senses of Self as indigenous people,133 but for
non-indigenous scholars, like myself, the drive for an indigenist
research agenda "evolves from a need to comprehend, resist, and
transform the crises related to the dual concerns of the effect that
colonization has had on Indigenous peoples and the ongoing erosion of
Indigenous languages, knowledge, and culture as a result of
colonization."134 In this way, indigenist research "borrows freely from
feminist research and critical approaches to research, but privileges
indigenous voices."135 For me, the desire to help decolonize research
through this dissertation stems from: recognition that the Euro-centric
theoretical traditions of 130 Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 36. 131 Battiste 2000,
xvii. 132 Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 35. 133 Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 38. 134
Battiste 2000, xx-xxi. 135 Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 147. 50 academia offer an
incomplete understanding of the social and natural worlds;
acknowledgment of the historic and contemporary harms inflicted on
indigenous peoples by the convergence of state practices and mainstream
scholarship; and understanding that circumpolar research is empirically
incomplete without incorporating the views of Arctic Indigenous peoples.

#### Settler colonialism defines social and political relations that normalizes ongoing extraction, dispossession, and dehumanization 

**Desrochers-Turgeon, 2021**

(Emelie - Vanier Scholar and a PhD student at the Azrieli School of
Architecture & Urbanism, Carleton University, "There Is a Breach in the
Wall: Encountering Arctic Terrains," June 2021,
https://averyreview.com/issues/53/breach-in-the-wall)

[[Set]{.mark}tler [col]{.mark}onialism]{.underline} here
[[is]{.underline}]{.mark} explored as [[a social formation]{.mark}: as
an imaginary, an attitude, and [an orientation that practices
taking]{.mark}, fixing, and apportioning]{.underline}.9 Architecture
represents the materialization of such social formation through the ways
[it [normalizes]{.mark} the [occupation]{.mark} of
land]{.underline}---what can be called "settler colonial
earth-writing"---[[through]{.mark} paving, irrigating, leveling,
cutting, digging[, extracting]{.mark}, displacing, [and securing
grounds.]{.mark}]{.underline} Architecture enshrines occupation by its
highly literal presence on terrains where existing relationships are
materially disrupted. As Heather Davis and Zoe Todd write,
[[set]{.mark}tler [col]{.mark}onialism "[is a severing of
relations]{.mark} between humans and the soil, between plants and
animals, between minerals and our bones.]{.underline}"10

Settler colonial earth-writing does not only operate materially.
[[Settlers developed]{.mark} complex [fictions to erase Indigenous
peoples' rights]{.mark} to their land [under their own legal
systems]{.mark} and worldviews, appropriating Indigenous territory under
the legal doctrine of terra nullius]{.underline}. [[These fictions
dehumanized Indigenous communities in order to reconcile the colonial
imaginary of empty land and the embodied experience of
settlement]{.underline}]{.mark}.11 The spatial descriptions that emerge
from these stories reflect the spatial illiteracy of colonists who,
without their customary units to "measure" the land, disregarded foreign
soil as a complex materiality brimming with cultural history. Emilie
Cameron argues [that [settler representations of the
arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} [continuously [nourish a sense of settlers
as innocent witnesses to the suffering of]{.mark} northern [Indigenous
peoples]{.mark} in order [to secure]{.mark} northern [resource
extraction and land]{.mark} [claims]{.mark}.]{.underline}12 Per Cameron,
these settler stories or fictions matter in the sense that they are
relational and material ordering practices that characterize settlers'
relationship to people and land.13

The soil itself is a major medium through which these complex fictions
manifest. In Dubravka Sekulić, Milica Tomić, and Philipp Sattler's
account of soil as an archive, "[[colonial
practices]{.underline}]{.mark}, having spread across territories and
soils, and once confined to the colonial context, [[continue to inform
our ideas of minerals]{.mark}, species, race, and gender, [permeating
our behavior]{.mark}, institutions, and imaginary to the present
day]{.underline}."14 On Turtle Island, [one enduring colonial fiction is
that of the pristine "north]{.underline}." Sherill Grace notes how
numerous disciplines have been mesmerized by the "north" and how it has
inspired countless authors.15 Robert Kroetsch writes: "\[t\]o write is,
in some metaphoric sense, to go North. To go North is, in some
metaphoric sense, to write."16 His consideration of the "north" and the
act of writing as a mutual metaphor is strong in the colonial
imagination. Comparing the white page to a virgin space, a terra nullia,
to write upon, suggests that the "north" is waiting to be written.
Geopoetic space par excellence, the "north," proves fertile for the
imaginations and fantasies of architects, engineers, and planners.

#### It is the central force from which other interlocking forms of oppression materialize and extend 

**Layden et al, 2025**

(Tamara -- PhD Candidate in Ecology at Colorado State University,
Dominique M. David-Chavez - Director of the Indigenous Land & Data
Stewards Lab & Professor of Indigenous Natural Resource Stewardship at
Colorado State University, Emma Galofré García, Gemara L. Gifford, Anna
Lavoie, Erin R. Weingarten, Sara P. Bombaci, "Confronting colonial
history: toward healing, just, and equitable Indigenous conservation
futures," Ecology & Society 30.1, 2025,
https://ecologyandsociety.org/vol30/iss1/art33/)

As concerns for the health and well-being of our planet and all its
relatives (both human and more-than-human) continue to rise, collective
calls for environmental and social justice are culminating. There is
growing agreement that [[the issues affecting our planet are not]{.mark}
scattered and [isolated, but]{.mark} systemic, [deeply]{.mark} rooted,
and [interconnected]{.mark}]{.underline} across diverse institutions,
disciplines, and practices. [Scholars and activists have identified
historic and ongoing [colonial systems of oppression]{.mark} as
[driv]{.mark}ing many of the [social and environmental
disparities]{.mark} we see today]{.underline} (Tuck and Yang 2012, Whyte
2018a, Gilio-Whitaker 2019, Eichler and Baumeister 2021, Curley et al.
2022). [These colonial [systems of oppression, which
