#### Vote AFF for a fugitive resistance \-\-- disrupting setter cartographies and foregrounding Indigenous relationalities is a method for both undoing dominant settler narratives about the Arctic and opening space for ongoing resistance 

**Nielsen 25** -- Kristine Nielsen, 2025, Fugitive Aesthetics in Sámi
Artists Lena Stenberg's Exhibition Borders (2023) and Pauliina
Feodoroff's Performance Matriarchy (2022),
https://doi.org/10.1080/02560046.2024.2430475, pp. 1-12, DOA: 7/14/2025,
//shubh

Fugitive Aesthetics in Sámi Artists Lena Stenberg's Exhibition Borders
(2023) and Pauliina Feodoroff's Performance Matriarchy (2022) Kristine
Nielsen Art History, Illinois Wesleyan University, Chicago, IL, USA
ABSTRACT How does contemporary Sámi visual art address land rights,
sovereignty and reclaim Sámi histories? This paper explores the work of
two contemporary Sámi artists, Lena Stenberg's exhibition Gränser
(Borders), from 2023, and Pauliina Feodoroff's performance Matriarchy,
staged at the 2022 Venice Biennale. The essay views their work through
the lens of a "fugitive aesthetic," a concept that defines a resistance
to Settler colonial practices through escape from dominant narratives.
Stenberg and Feodoroff confront colonial borders and return the land to
the Sámi pictorially and, in Feodoroff's case, materially through her
decolonial project with the non-profit organisation Snowchange. Their
defiant "escape" takes place from within the institutions of the
contemporary artworld but with the goal of disrupting their containment
and prioritising Sámi sovereignty. KEYWORDS Sámi; Lena Stenberg;
Pauliina Feodoroff; Matriarchy; Borders; fugitive aesthetic; resistance;
sovereignty How does contemporary Sámi visual art engage with ideas of
sovereignty and land rights and what artistic strategies might help
advance these goals? The work of two contempor- ary Sámi artists, Lena
Stenberg and Pauliina Feodoroff, will demonstrate distinct visual
proposals for ways to culturally reclaim and sustain Sámi ways of living
and knowing. Lena Stenberg's solo exhibit Gränser (Borders) was first
shown at the Nordic House in the Faroe Islands (20 February--5 March
2023) and then at the Sámi Center for Contem- porary Art/Sámi
Dáiddaguovddáš (SDG) in Kárášjohka/Karasjok, Norway (22 April--18 June
2023). Stenberg employs three-dimensional and two-dimensional work to
address Sámi lived experiences and reestablish the presence of her
Gárasavvon/Karesuando ancestors forced to relocate due to the creation
of national borders by the Nordic nation states. Skolt Sámi artist
Pauliina Feodoroff's project Matriarchy, a performance at the Sámi
Pavilion at the 2022 Venice Biennale, advocates for and, in fact,
implements decolonialism by protecting the Arctic woodlands of Sápmi.
Sámi political scientist Rauna Kuokkanen describes Nordic colonialism as
Settler colonialism characterised by the steady and expanding takeover
of Sámi land and resources through policies enacted by a dominant
Settler population (Kuokkanen 2020, 512). In contrast to external
colonial domination of an Indigenous peoples © 2025 Critical Arts
CONTACT Kristine Nielsen knielse1@iwu.edu Art History, Illinois Wesleyan
University, 1312 Park Street, Bloo- mington, IL 61701, USA CRITICAL ARTS
https://doi.org/10.1080/02560046.2024.2430475 through violence, Settler
colonialism may use a gradual process of Indigenous erasure through
institutional policies and systems that require their assimilation.
Viewing Sámi contemporary [art through]{.underline} the [lens of
**Settler colonialism** invites an **[exploration]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}
a "**[fugitive aesthetic]{.mark}**,"]{.underline} a term described by
scholars of Indigenous studies Jarret Marti- neau and Eric Ritskes as
[[resisting]{.mark} full **assimilation** to **[colonial]{.mark}
[thinking]{.mark}.**]{.underline} Indigenous visual art [[**interrupts**
and **unsettles**]{.mark} the **[colonial
narrative]{.mark}**]{.underline} by providing a "fugitive Indi- geneity"
that creatively negates a Settler colonial worldview (Martineau and
Ritskes 2014, ii, iv). The term "[[fugitive]{.underline}]{.mark}" [has
its own history in [**scholarship** denoting]{.mark} escape, nomadism,
and **[resistance]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Film and media scholars Bruce
Bennett and Katarzyna Mar- ciniak, for example, frame refugee
experiences depicted in cinema as a fugitive aes- thetic that engages
with bordered thinking, flight, and migration (Bennett and Marciniak
2022). Earlier use of the term fugitive derives from Black studies
critically analysing postcolonialism and histories of slavery. Here
fugivity concern acts of refusal, evasion, and resistance aiming to
escape structures of oppression. Activist Stefano Harney and cultural
theorist Fred Moten, for example, use the term fugivity to portray
practices and spaces that live beyond the reach of formal institutions,
such as academia, and invite alternative ways of thinking and being
(Harney and Moten 2013, 25--43). \[F\]ugivity is being separate from
settling. It is a being in motion that has learned that 'organ- izations
are obstacles to organising oneselves' and that there are spaces and
modalities that exist separate from the logical, logistical, the housed
and the positioned, as stated in their book's foreword (Harney and Moten
2013, 11). Here [fugivity operates [as]{.mark} a [position]{.mark} [to
**stake out**]{.mark} physically or **[conceptually]{.mark}**
[that]{.mark} [escapes **dominant narratives** by **creating
spaces**]{.mark} [beyond the existent ones]{.mark}.]{.underline}
Martineau and Ritskes' concept of fugitive Indi- geneity brings in the
visual arts through the creative refusal to comply to a colonial
mindset. In a fugitive aesthetic, the visual artist flees from
institutional containment and commodification, deliberately avoiding
assimilation into the spaces of dominant culture. [They sustain
Indigenous identities and memories that resonate for the Indigenous
communities rather than an external audience]{.underline}. Thus, while
nomadism, displacement, and bordered thinking are key concepts in the
work of Stenberg and Feodoroff, I will argue that a fugitive aesthetic
conceived as creative negation proves equally important to their work.
While scholars have placed Stenberg's artistic practice in the context
of art history and identity (Grini 2017) or globalisation and local
spatial identities (Bergmann 2014), scholars on Feodoroff's performance
Matriarchy have focused on its feminist and envir- onmentalist advocacy
and claim for independence (García-Antón 2022) including its subversion
of the capitalist consumption of resources (Williamson 2023). This essay
contributes to current scholarship by viewing Stenberg's work Mobility
(2014) and Laavu (2019) and Feodoroff's Matriarchy (2022) through the
lens of a fugitive aesthetic of escape, Sámi relational memory, and the
importance of place. These two artists con- front colonial borders and
return the land to the Sámi pictorially and, in Feodoroff's case,
materially through her decolonial project with the non-profit
organisation Snow- change. Their defiant "escape" takes place from
within institutions of the contemporary artworld but with the goal of
disrupting their containment and prioritising Sámi sovereignty. 2 K.
NIELSEN Resistance through a Sámi worldview: Lena Stenberg's Borders
Sámi artist Lena Stenberg (b. 1961), originally from Leavas Sameby
outside Giron/Kiruna, in northern Sweden, uses [sculpture, installation,
and photomontage to explore themes of home and migration]{.underline}.
She belongs to the Gárasavvon/Karesuando Sámi, raised in a family of
reindeer herders closely tied to the land and natural environment. Her
most recent exhibition Gränser (Borders, 2023) [[renders]{.mark}
**[intimate]{.mark}** the impact of **[national border con-
struction]{.mark}**]{.underline} between Norway and Finland in the early
20th century and its effect on her Sámi ancestors. It explores her
family's connection and disconnection to the Sápmi areas, now in Finnish
and Norwegian territories, and how Settler colonialism and nation-state
building severed their ties to the land. Her artistic process involves
[[appro- priating]{.mark} old photographs from ethnographic [archives to
**reconfigure** them]{.mark}]{.underline}. Using photo- montage, she
adds the photographs of her ancestors to the picture plane, usually in a
depicted Sámi landscape, and [[**metaphorically** corrects
**colonial**]{.mark} **histories** and [erasures]{.mark}.]{.underline}
Earlier Sámi artists have also sought to redress family history,
including Katarina Pirak Sikku's lengthy project Nammaláhpán (2014).
Where Sikku explores the photographic archives of Sámi ancestors in
Uppsala's State Institute of Race Biology, Nils-Aslak Valkea- pää
reclaims ethnographic photographs of Sámi ancestors from European
archives in his 1988 book Beaivi, áhčážan (The Sun, My Father). Their
artistic processes involve gathering pictorial fragments to restore a
shattered collective memory. In contrast to her two- dimensional work,
the implications of a fugitive aesthetic emerges more strongly, I
believe, in her three-dimensional projects, which resemble
[**[functional objects]{.mark}**, such as a sleigh with luggage,
[conveying]{.mark} **[mobility]{.mark}** [and]{.mark}
**[displacement]{.mark}**]{.underline}, or the tent called lávvu in
Northern Sámi. The conical shape of Stenberg's wooden sculpture
Mobilitet (Mobility, 2014) (Figure 1), also featured in her Borders
exhibit, represents the Sámi tent lávvu. The lávvu, typically covered
with reindeer hides and, more recently, with heavy fabric, serves as a
mobile shelter for the nomadic Sámi, as they journey alongside their
herds of domesticated rein- deer. But the tent represented in Mobility
lacks cover and therefore proper shelter as a domicile. Instead, its
[open frame [invites]{.mark} [investigation]{.mark} [of]{.mark} the
[structure]{.mark} and scattered com- ponents [attached]{.mark}
[to]{.mark} the **[vertical poles]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Upside down
and sideways wooden chairs, stools, utensils, side tables, and a canoe
paddle interlock with vertical poles using mortise and tenon joints. The
furnishings appear weightless and mobile compared to the load- bearing
poles critical for tent's stability and durability. While some of the
objects, such as the paddle, reflect items important for Sámi
communities living near rivers and lakes where fishing and
transportation by water are essential for daily life and survival, other
objects convey furniture from a more stationary abode. A side table, for
instance, derives from a life of permanence in modernity. Notably,
however, since the floating fur- niture and household items do not
contribute to the structural integrity or essential func- tionality of
the tent, they point to their superfluous, if not cluttering existence.
Western tourism and histories of nationalism have fabricated an image of
the tent, including both the lávvu and the Native American tepee, as
signifying metonymically the Indigenous as a collective. In this
colonial visual language, the lávvu is easily substi- tuted for the Sámi
people. The title Mobility leads the mind toward a Sámi nomadic life-
style of reindeer herding represented by the lávvu. Moveability defines
the nomadic lifestyle, requiring a functionally compact dwelling to
easily adapt to life on the move. CRITICAL ARTS 3 But I argue that
Stenberg's sculpture conceptually reverses what one might deem mobile
and transient, too. Even if most Sámi no longer live nomadically, it
remains relevant as a frame of reference. "A modern nomad is what I am.
My home is within me," says Stenberg. "We probably still carry that
mobility within us" (Stenberg 2014, 74--75). Stenberg further references
Nils Aslak Valkeapää's 1985 book Ruoktu Váimmus (Trekways of the Wind),
which addresses how one's home refers to the landscape, one's
experiences, spirituality and culture. I contend that Stenberg's work
Mobility conveys how the Sámi tent serves as the very foundation, the
backbone for the concept of home in a Sámi collective ima- ginary,
visually evidenced by the load-bearing poles in the sculpture. Because
the often-modern furnishings in her sculpture appear weightless,
decorative, non-supportive, and therefore inconsequential, they suggest
that we reconsider the weight of these items in a Sámi mindset.
[Mobility evokes not only a transient reindeer herding lifestyle but
also a fundamental Sámi **[worldview]{.mark}** [functioning]{.mark}
[as]{.mark} the [underlying structural support]{.mark} [for]{.mark} the
**[conception of home]{.mark}**]{.underline}. The sculpture is therefore
both tent and vision of the world, an inner landscape and enduring
presence. More felt than formally acknowledged, [a **resist- ance**
[against]{.mark} **[Settler colonialism]{.mark}** resides]{.underline}
in all her work. Stenberg's exhibition Borders was first shown at the
Nordic House in the Faroe Islands (20 February--5 March 2023) which held
a series of ten exhibitions entitled Sápmi Stories showcasing thirteen
Sámi artists (Sissel Mutale Bergh, Outi Pieski, Birit Haarla, Katja
Haarla, Lena Stenberg, Máret Ánne Sara, Anders Sunna, Michiel Brouwer,
Katarina Pirak Sikku, Liselotte Wajstedt, Lisa Vipola, Hanne Grete
Einarsen and Tomas Colbengtson). The themes of the series concerned art
history's "monocultural worldview" and how Figure 1. Lena Stenberg,
Mobilitet, 2014, wood (Photo credit: Lena Stenberg). 4 K. NIELSEN
Eurocentric art history traps the concept of beauty in an "exclusionary
system" of aes- thetics that omits minority cultures such as the Sámi
(Sørensen 2022, 11). Art history tends to relegate Sámi art to the realm
of Indigenous art or folklore, says the curator, hereby confining art
through language. To counteract this confinement, the Nordic House asked
the invited Sámi artists to expand our ways of experiencing art
(Sørensen 2022, 12). Stenberg's work speaks to this diversion between a
Eurocentric art historical vision focused on dominant culture and a Sámi
challenge to this structure, as I will demonstrate next with her second
sculptural iteration of the lávvu, from 2019. Lavvu (Figure 2) is made
of red-painted metal with only the structural poles visible. It appears
like a sturdy armature for a tent. Its brightly coloured, stream-lined
and indust- rially-made form removes the sculpture from its traditional
wooden material, evoking a mass-produced commodity instead, like a
modular, pre-fabricated home personalised through colour choice. In the
gallery space, Lavvu's principal reference speaks to art his- tory's
modern aesthetic with its clean lines and primary colour, mimicking the
formal language of modern art. Looking at Lavvu again, however, one
notices how two pole lines extend beyond the body of the sculpture and
onto the floor uniting in a looped metal honda knot. From this
viewpoint, the clasp turns into a slider and the curved lines of the red
metal transform into a lasso in motion creating the illusion of a line
in the air made by the lasso. The lávvu and lasso are hereby visually
and conceptually joined in association and function: The lávvu now
evokes the lasso used in the summer- time and autumn when Sámi reindeer
herders catch the calves with their lassos to mark them (Lena Stenberg,
email to author, March 29, 2024). Where the lassos used to be made of
rope, today they are brightly coloured plastic, as represented by the
red colour of Figure 2. Lena Stenberg, Lavvu, 2019, painted metal (Photo
credit: Lena Stenberg). CRITICAL ARTS 5 Lavvu. Red, alongside yellow, is
also the colour that symbolically moulds the Sámi land and Sámi being,
as Sámi scholar Liisa-Rávná Finbog notes (Finbog 2024, 54). Stenberg's
sculpture thus references a tent, a modern work of art, and a memory
archive of Sámi embodied events. Lavvu [**[expands]{.mark}** our
**[perceptions]{.mark}** of what we are experiencing, [unfolding]{.mark}
a **[creative rupture]{.mark}** that leads us [to]{.mark} a [**tacit
realm** that draws on **place**]{.mark}, **memory**, **relations**,
[and]{.mark} **[practices]{.mark}**.]{.underline} Viewers knowledgable
about Sámi reindeer herding find themselves trans- ported from the
gallery space to the Sápmi community. It is a [**[fugitive
move]{.mark}**, a flight [away from]{.mark} a **[confined
aesthetic]{.mark}** and [toward]{.mark} a **[space of freedom]{.mark}**
that **cannot** be recuperated by the gallery or "**European aesthetic
paradigms**,"]{.underline} to use the words of Martineau and Ritskes
(2014, ix). Lavvu transforms before our very eyes the moment one thinks
from and with Sámi ways of knowing and living, changing sense
perceptions from the act of looking to remembering embodied, tactile,
and relational Sámi experiences. Place lies at the very heart of
Stenberg's work and strategy in terms of exhibition sites and subject
matter. She chose to exhibit Borders on the Faroe Islands, an autonomous
ter- ritory within the Kingdom of Denmark subject to a history of Danish
colonialism, and later at the Sámi Center for Contemporary Art/Sámi
Dáiddaguovddáš in Kárášjohka/Karasjok. Moreover, much of her work takes
place outside the gallery space and in nature. For example, her 2023
work Nomad, built and situated outside, represents an old Sámi settle-
ment's drying racks with braided surfaces. She views the act of creating
as deeply con- nected to the land and derived from Sámi knowledge
systems. Even when Stenberg approaches the concept of place from within
the art institution, such as in her Borders exhibit, she explores ideas
of dwelling and displacement, including the forced relocations of her
Gárasavvon/Karesuando ancestors and the photographs of them taken by
tourists and anthropologists in Romssavággi/Tromsdalen between 1860 and
1930 (Stenberg 2022). Borders reflects critically on a Nordic history of
border building that began with the Lapp Codicil of 1751: The Codicil
was an appendix to the initial border treaty between the Swedish-Finnish
and Danish-Norwegian kingdoms, which honoured the nomadic Sámi's rights
to move freely across national borders (Institutet för språk och folk-
minnen n.d.). But tensions between reindeer herding and agriculture grew
increasingly with Settler colonialism in the early 19th century. By
1852, Russia had sealed the border between Norway and Finland resulting
in the Sámi losing access to pastures and fishing on the Arctic coast.
Then, in 1889, Russia closed the Swedish-Finnish border to the nomadic
Sámi. In the 1920s and 1930s, Sweden further displaced many Northern
Sámi southward, a process referred to as bággojohtin (Institutet för
språk och folkminnen n.d.). Stenberg's relatives were among those
displaced, and [Borders poignantly highlights these
displacements]{.underline}. She layers photographs of Northern Sámi
individuals, often relatives, alongside their geographic locations in
two-dimensional spaces, at times arranging the space in a chessboard
pattern, symbolising the strategic pursuit of Sápmi lands for con- quest
(Figure 3). Stenberg focuses on cultural survival of the Sámi and
visually restores her relatives' presence in Sápmi in a way that
resonates with, yet differs significantly, from Sámi artist Pauliina
Feodoroff's project Matriarchy to which I now turn. Pauliina Feodoroff's
decolonial project Matriarchy Artist, filmmaker, theatre director, and
writer Pauliina Feodoroff (b. 1977) is a Skolt Sámi from Inari in the
Finnish (Če'vetjäu'rr) and Russian (Suõ'nnjel) regions of Sápmi. As the
6 K. NIELSEN former President of the Saami Council, Feodoroff has been
actively engaged in Sámi land and water access rights both as a
spokesperson and activist. She [combines art and stew- ardship of the
land reflecting a Sámi holistic **worldview** [centred on relations and
**mutual exchange**]{.mark} [between **people** and]{.mark} the
**[land]{.mark}**]{.underline} (García-Antón 2022, 5--6). In 2020, the
Office of Contemporary Art Norway commissioned Feodoroff to exhibit at
the Nordic Pavilion recast as the "Sámi Pavilion" for the 2022 Venice
Biennale. The curators offered the primary space to three artists Máret
Ánne Sara, Anders Sunna, and Feodoroff who would have the chance to
"claim a sovereign space" symbolically, states co-curator Liisa-Rávná
Finbog (Office of Contemporary Art Norway n.d.). The opportunity to
appeal to art collectors and museums at the Venice Biennale led
Feodoroff to accept the commission. At the Sámi Pavilion, Feodoroff
showed Matriarchy (2022, 90 min), a performance in three acts with seven
performances in total for the duration of the Venice Biennale season.
The opening act, entitled "First Contact," begins with a procession of
six Sámi women exhibiting to the audience outside of the Sámi Pavilion
their duodji, or Sámi hand- crafts representing elements of Sámi
knowledge and traditions (Figure 4). They show the audience various
objects and foods integral to Sámi ways of living such as grass and
lichen, an algae-fungi that reindeer graze on (Rubin 2022). Once the
performers move inside the pavilion, they place the duodji gifts on the
floor, and two women dance in silence in a choreography that evokes a
back-and-forth exchange but also suggests Figure 3. Lena Stenberg,
Schack Bröder, 2023, 25 parts. Print on plywood. 2023 (Photo credit:
Lena Stenberg). CRITICAL ARTS 7 colonial dominance. For example, one of
the dancers dressed in all-black may represent a crow standing in for
the colonial nation, as Norwegian art historian Camila Mørk Røstvik has
suggested (Røstvik 2023, 395). Eventually, additional dancers arrive
with long ribbons on banners, and the energy of the performance
intensifies with the addition of sound. Music by Sámi musicians Anna
Morottaja, 169 (Mio Negga), and Mari Boine provides the backdrop for
this part of the performance turned suggestively political. Boine's 1994
song "Mun Da'han Lean Oaivámuš" ("I Am in Command") concerns borders and
the effort to establish control when these borders are ignored or
breached. Like in Sten- berg's exhibition Borders, the song and
Feodoroff's performance denounce the borders that arose with the
building of nation states, which displaced Sámi families and obstructed
access to their livelihoods. "First Contact" initially imagines a
mutually respect- ful meeting between the Indigenous and recent Settlers
but closes on an agitated note when Settler colonialism breaks these
relations. In part two, "Auction," the performers stage a mock auction
of landscape portraits with a real auction happening offsite for museums
and collectors. The landscapes depict Indi- genous territories and
reindeer habitats threatened by deforestation and mining, particu- larly
in Skolt Sámi regions but also areas in North America. The auction
offers the landscape portraits as art for sale, encouraging collectors
and institutions to acquire Figure 4. Pauliina Feodoroff, Matriarchy,
2022, performance (Photo credit: Andreas Haglund). 8 K. NIELSEN the art,
not the land. The actual auction ushers the purchaser into a novel
relationship with the artist by establishing a dual obligation of care
and support. In this arrangement, the buyer receives the landscape
portrait and scheduled visitation rights to the land, while committing
to its preservation and rehabilitation, hereby becoming an ally in Sámi
prin- ciples of reciprocity and stewardship (García-Antón 2022, 12).
When Feodoroff's perform- ance was not taking place, the Sámi Pavilion
exhibited the landscape portraits as videos. In the videos, the camera
slowly tracks the landscapes captured by Sámi artist Outi Pieski, with
the scenes narrated in English and representing a Sámi collective "we"
(the land and associated guardians) imploring the viewer to support the
rewilding project (Office of Contemporary Art Norway n.d.). The third
and final part, titled "Matriarchy," affirms [rematri]{.mark}ation as
key to envisioning a sovereign Sámi future. [[Indigenous
**matriarchies**]{.mark} are societies that centre around the mother,
including Mother Earth, and emphasise **matrilineal qualities** by
[prioritis]{.mark}in[g]{.mark} the values of **[equality]{.mark}**,
peace, [and]{.mark} a **[reciprocal duty of care]{.mark}**.]{.underline}
In part three, a gathering of Sámi women and their allies engage in
collective healing through actions that reject the colonial inheritance
by leading through nurture. "It has always been the female that lead,"
Feodoroff's father says, "The Reindeer, the people, always the older
female" (Feodoroff 2022, 187). [The **[framework]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}
**[rematriation]{.mark}** [accepts]{.mark} the [land not as property but
as a place for a **community**, underscoring a shift from]{.mark}
individual [possession to **rela- tional belonging**]{.mark} and
stewardship.]{.underline} Handwritten on a landscape portrait depicting
rein- deer and herders, Feodoroff's concluding words urge the audience:
"Don't buy land that is not yours" (Figure 5). Feodoroff's project at
the Venice Biennale not only displays Sámi landscape but---in a
fugitive, separate space beyond the art institution---it reclaims Sámi
land, and not as a metaphor. Her prior and concurrent land and river
projects effectively decolonialise the land through a novel strategy
that restores land governance to its original stewards through
acquisitions in the private market. Feodoroff works with the Snowchange
Coop- erative, a non-profit organisation that aims to shield northern
Finnish land from further deforestation. It has promoted the rewilding
of the land and the leveraging of Sámi knowledge for ecological
restoration since the 2010s (Feodoroff 2022, 237). Snowchange restores
the biodiversity and the Indigenous ownership in the area using an
initiative called the Landscape Rewilding Program. The decolonial
process includes also renaming the purchased lands in the Skolt Sámi
language. For example, the Skolt Sámi community renamed one site Čuhč
člåå'ddrääuhvää'r (A Hill Where the Capercaillie Will Be Left at Peace)
to restore livelihood to the Capercaillie, a forest bird with a
declining population due to the loss of its habitat in northern
coniferous forests (Rubin 2022). Loans and donations from the European
Investment Bank, Dutch bank Triodos, alongside donations from various
Finnish organisations, the Google Foundation, and Christensen Fund have
made it possible for Feodoroff and Snowchange to purchase private lands
in far northern Finland (Rubin 2022). At this point, Snowchange has
rewilded over 50000 hectares of land and has become Finland's
second-largest ecological restorer (Rubin 2022). Ultimately, the goal is
to return the land and forests to a Sámi community or village able to
carry out the oversight of the lands (Mustonen and Feodoroff 2021, 237).
Feodoroff's artistic strategy both engages with and disengages the
capitalist system. Her performance at the Venice Biennale strategises
the standpoint of viewing "art as capital, and currency as resistance,"
as Katya García-Antón, the former Director for the CRITICAL ARTS 9
Office of Contemporary Art Norway, states (García-Antón 2022, 13).
Accordingly, Feodoroff participates in the capitalist tactics of the
artworld: she is the artist selling her artwork to art museums and art
dealers, the Museum of Modern Art in Helsinki being the first of several
purchases of the landscape portraits. But her Venice Biennale proceeds
have supported Snowchange's purchase of 40 hectares of woodland (Rubin
2022). As a result, and in line with a fugitive aesthetic, she turns the
system on its head by purchasing Sápmi land with the monetary gains.
[The **[rupture]{.mark}** she generates [shifts]{.mark} [from]{.mark}
the [individual]{.mark} [to]{.mark} the [collective]{.mark}, from the
contemporary artworld to Sápmi, from com- modity to nature]{.underline},
with the goal of reinscribing Sámi presence legally both within and
outside the control of state institutions. While her decolonial project
escapes being fully contained by the art institutions and sus- tains
Sámi ways of living through decolonialism, challenges persist. The act
of reclaiming ter- ritory remains a struggle, because the Finnish
constitution continues to interfere in the connections between Sámi
families, animals, and lands. Private and state lands are the only two
forms of land ownership permitted in Finland today. Moreover, Finland
recognises the Sámi as an Indigenous peoples, but it does so without
affording them rights to tra- ditional livelihoods (Kuokkanen 2019,
88--89). It has yet to endorse the Indigenous rights Figure 5. Pauliina
Feodoroff, Matriarchy, 2022, performance (Photo credit: Andreas
Haglund). 10 K. NIELSEN outlined in the International Labour
Organisation (ILO) Convention 169 introduced by the United Nations. The
ILO 169 distinguishes between minority rights and Indigenous rights by
framing the latter in terms of collective rights of a people. Viewing
the Sámi as a collective would acknowledge the rights of Indigenous
peoples to their lands (Henriksen 2008, 10). But the Finnish government
views the Sámi in Finland as a very small ethnic minority, instead,
bereft of legal entitlement to reindeer herding rights or land rights
(Kuokkanen 2019, 79). Like Stenberg, Feodoroff is acutely aware of the
colonial history of Sápmi, which she traces to the year 1542 when the
Swedish King Gustav I Vasa subjugated the Finnish and Sámi wilderness
and traditional Sámi community of the siida or sijdd in Skolt Sámi. This
occupation then set the precedent for all the governments that followed,
so that the Sámi have effectively lost their land rights ever since
(Mustonen and Feodoroff 2021, 232). The Skolt Sámi have been caught in
border conflicts with neighbouring nations for centuries. When Finland
ceded to the Soviet Union in 1945, the Skolt Sámi either stayed in
Finnish Peäccam/Petsamo or relocated from their ancestral lands to start
a new life near Lake Aanaar/Inari (Feodoroff 2022, 170). Aside from the
shifting national borders, Settler colonial practices have affected the
Skolt Sámi areas through Soviet-imposed land collectivisation, nickel
mining, and Finnish forestry and river regulations, which have further
forced the Skolt Sámi off their land (García-Antón 2022, 5). Layered
upon this history are the local environmental disasters such as acid
rain and the contamination from Chernobyl, the aftermath of which
greatly affected Sápmi and Skolt land (García- Antón 2022, 6). These
historical and environmental factors propel the urgency of Feo- doroff's
artistic and activist practices in sustaining Sámi survival and
sovereignty. The struggle for sovereignty It is no coincidence that the
photographic landscape portrait of Sámi land figures as a key trope for
both Feodoroff and Stenberg. The concept of the landscape is in itself
an histori- cal invention closely tied to imperialism and power, as
exemplified by European land- scape portraits emerging alongside
colonialism in the 1600s to the 1800s (Mitchell 2002, 5--7). Similarly,
cartographic maps aided in territorial expansion by presenting Indi-
genous land as a blank slate. Sweden and Denmark-Norway, for example,
mapped Sápmi to establish territorial claims and secure trade routes
(Ojala and Nordin 2019, 99--100). Just as early modern mapmaking
practices omitted Sámi communities from their land depic- tions, so
today the Sámi are still often seen as barriers to ignore in the pursuit
of monetary initiatives such as mining and forestry. In the late 1800s
and throughout the 1900s, pho- tography served as the vehicle through
which ethnographers, scientists, missionaries, and tourists documented
the native Nordic landscape and lay visual claim to it. Taking the
photographic landscape, Stenberg and Feodoroff employ a fugitive
aesthetic by using the idea of the Western landscape against itself and
reinscribing Indigeneity in the act. Feodoroff's Matriarchy turns the
Western landscape portrait on its head by first appealing to a European
aesthetic paradigm of fine art objects and then instrumentalising the
pur- chase of the landscape portrait as a social contract. In this
social contract, the purchaser acquires the picture and gains scheduled
visitation rights to the land but, importantly, also pledges to preserve
and restore it. Resistance lies in the very act of unconventionally
generating a social contract of care within the structure of capitalism.
Stenberg's Borders project traces the footsteps of her Sámi ancestors
and their habitats through her CRITICAL ARTS 11 photomontages. Her
black-and-white montage Giððajohtin (Spring Migration), 2023, shows Sámi
ancestors before their forced relocations, but it is the landscape that
domi- nates. She pictorially restores the land to its original owners
using the historical photo- graphs derived from the archives of
anthropology and tourism of the late 19th and early 20th centuries. I
have demonstrated how two, contemporary Sámi artists go beyond reviving
Sámi culture by applying creative strategies that expose power dynamics
and escape from being subsumed by colonial thinking. [They imagine an
elsewhere, a separate space, **[defined by resistance]{.mark}** and a
sense of freedom and mobility.]{.underline} Feodoroff circumvents the
art- world's monetary and object focus by acquiring Sámi land piece by
piece, opening an opportunity for these very institutions to enter a
social contract with Sámi communities. Stenberg prevents her art from
being fully contained by the gallery, creating an elsewhere of
alternative experiences resistant to the European aesthetic paradigm.
Both artists choose to reside in Sápmi, often working in nature and away
from the global art scene. Their independence is indicative of a greater
development in contemporary Sámi art where artists increasingly bypass
institutional intermediaries to establish independent platforms for
themselves through access to production. Nonetheless, the progress
remains challenging. Sámi platforms, such as The Sámi Center for
Contemporary Art, still rely on federal support and larger funding
initiatives that retain a relationship of dependence (Hætta 2024, 128).
Moreover, the threat of reappropriation remains for Sámi artists and
their work. Máret Ánne Sara, for example, challenged land exploitation
and reindeer herding policies when she installed her work Pile o' Sápmi
outside the Indre Finnmark District Court in Norway in 2016. However,
since then, the risk of her work's co-optation by cultural institutions
requires Sara's consistent navigation. Pauliina Feodoroff's Matriarchy
is no exception either. The Venice Biennale platform provides her with
access to the dealers and museums with purchasing power, but she must
plea for their involvement, effectively reinforcing a colonial dynamic.
Amid these challenges, artists like Feodoroff and Stenberg continue to
[[move **beyond the bounds**]{.mark} and compre- hension [of]{.mark} the
[colonial]{.mark} [mindset]{.mark} and toward **reinscribing** Sámi
presence in Sápmi [through]{.mark} a **[fugitive
aesthetic]{.mark}**.]{.underline}

#### It's [try]{.underline}-or-[die]{.underline} to center [Indigenous]{.underline} [worldmaking]{.underline} in [deliberative]{.underline} forums.

**Greaves 16** -- Wilfrid William John Greaves, Associate professor of
international relations at the University of Victoria, 2016,
Constructing In/Security in the Arctic: Polar Politics, Indigenous
Peoples, and Environmental Change in Canada and Norway,
https://www.proquest.com/docview/1820914461/, pp. 50-53, DOA: 7/6/2025,

**[[Decolonization]{.underline}]{.mark}** -- [in]{.underline} this [case
of how in/security is understood in the **circumpolar
Arctic**]{.underline} -- is thus an analytical and normative goal of
this project. It is important to note, however, that decolonization does
not and cannot mean returning to a precolonial reality; the cognitive,
material, and affective legacies of colonialism may be repaired, but
they can never be undone. As such, decolonization "does not mean and has
not meant a total rejection of all theory or research or Western
knowledge. Rather, [it is [about]{.mark} **[centring
\[indigenous]{.mark}**\] concerns and **[worldviews]{.mark}** and then
coming to know and understand **[theory and research]{.mark}** from
\[indigenous\] perspectives and [for]{.mark} **\[[indigenous\]
purposes]{.mark}**]{.underline}."136 Indigenist research seeks to bring
indigenous peoples' views back into the realm of knowledge production
from which they have typically been excluded. Such an understanding of
the possibility and limits of decolonization will not satisfy those who
call for decolonization to be applied literally to settlercolonial
political, social, and economic systems and institutions currently
extant on Indigenous lands, and who decry the use of "decolonization as
metaphor ... because it turns decolonization into an empty signifier to
be filled by any track towards liberation." These critics argue that
"decolonization in the settler colonial context must involve the
repatriation of land simultaneous to recognition of how land and
recognition of land have always been differently understood and enacted;
that is, all the land, and not just symbolically."137 On such terms,
decolonizing the foreign and security policies of settler-colonial
states is an oxymoron; real decolonization would involve fully
disassembling settler societies' power over indigenous peoples through
the restoration of all settler-occupied territories to the descendants
of their original inhabitants. This literal view of decolonization is
rejected in this dissertation as neither a useful starting point nor
even a desirable objective for political or academic praxis. Such a
maximalist and literal conception of decolonization elides profound
normative questions 136 Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 39. 137 Eve Tuck and K.
Wayne Yang, "Decolonization is Not a Metaphor," Decolonization:
Indigeneity, Education and Society 1, no. 1 (2012): 7. Emphasis in
original. 51 regarding the future of native-born settler descendants and
more recent immigrants inhabiting indigenous lands, and offers no
reasonable starting point for political negotiation, cooperation, and
reconciliation between indigenous and non-indigenous peoples. Instead,
this dissertation employs an understanding of decolonization that
aspires towards a post-colonial politics capable of guiding social
scientific inquiry and informing meaningful political change, rather
than contemplating an impossible restoration of pre-colonial reality. It
seeks to bring the voices and views of Indigenous peoples in the Arctic
to the same page as the perspectives of Arctic states, incorporating the
original inhabitants of the circumpolar region into a discourse of
Arctic security from which they have been excluded. In outlining
possible parameters for an indigenist research agenda, Tuhiwai Smith
identifies 25 different projects that comprise a comprehensive research
program for indigenous peoples. The projects have both empirical and
methodological goals, namely to incorporate indigenous knowledge and
indigenous knowledge production into academic research: The projects are
not claimed to be entirely indigenous or to have been created by
indigenous researchers. Some approaches have arisen out of social
science methodologies, which in turn have arisen out of methodological
issues raised by research with various oppressed groups. Some projects
invite multidisciplinary research approaches. Others have arisen
directly out of indigenous practices ... Most fall well within what will
be recognized as empirical research, \[but\] not all do.138 As a
non-indigenous person, and moreover as a privileged, male member of
settler-colonial society in Canada, one of the Arctic states under
examination in this dissertation, some of these research projects are
unavailable to me. It is neither possible nor would it be appropriate
for me to engage in claiming, naming, or giving testimony to indigenous
experiences. Other projects such as revitalizing, restoring, and
returning indigenous practices, language, culture, or territory are
beyond the scope of this dissertation. However, elements of three
indigenist projects are contained within this research, and can be
meaningfully and appropriately contributed to by me as a non-indigenous
scholar examining indigenous political issues: Indigenizing consists of
two dimensions: "The first one is similar to that which has occurred in
literature with a centring of the landscapes, images, languages, themes,
metaphors, and stories in the indigenous world ... \[The second\]
centres a politics of 138 Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 142-143. 52 indigenous
identity and indigenous cultural action ... grounded in the alternative
conceptions of world view and value systems."139 Representing: "The
representing project spans both the notion of representation as a
political concept and representation as a form of voice and expression.
In the political sense colonialism specifically excluded indigenous
peoples from any form of decision making. State and governments have
long made decisions hostile to the interests of indigenous communities
... [Being able as a **minimum right** to **[voice]{.mark}** the views
and opinions of [indigenous]{.mark} communities [in]{.mark} **various**
**[decision-making bodies]{.mark}** is still being struggled
over]{.underline}."140 Reframing: "[**Reframing** is about
[taking]{.mark} much **[greater control]{.mark}** [over]{.mark} the
[ways in which **indigenous issues**]{.mark} [and]{.mark} **[social
problems]{.mark}** [are]{.mark} **[discussed]{.mark}** [and]{.mark}
**[handled]{.mark}**]{.underline} ... The framing of an issue is about
making decisions about its parameters, about what is in the foreground,
what is in the background, and what shadings or complexities exist
within the frame. [The project of reframing is related to **[defining
the problem]{.mark}** or issue [and]{.mark} [determining]{.mark}
[how]{.mark} best **[to solve]{.mark}** that problem]{.underline}."141
To be sure, this dissertation presents detailed examinations of the
Arctic policies of two circumpolar states. As such, it could be said to
(re)produce the conceptions of Arctic in/security employed by settler
societies that have marginalized, instrumentalized, and oppressed
Indigenous peoples in the region. However, the dissertation also
provides the first detailed case-based and comparative analysis of the
views of Arctic Indigenous peoples towards the meaning of in/security in
their homelands. In doing so, it builds upon and contributes to recent
literature, especially in Canada, that centres indigenous peoples in the
foreign and security policies and practices of the states in which they
reside.142 This project thus reframes the way in which 139 Tuhiwai Smith
1999, 146. 140 Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 150. 141 Tuhiwai Smith 1999, 153. 142
J. Marshall Beier, International Relations in Uncommon Places:
Indigeneity, Cosmology, and the Limits of International Theory (New
York: Palgrave, 2005); Beier 2007; Leanne Broadhead, "Canadian
Sovereignty versus Northern Security: The Case for Updating Our Mental
Map of the Arctic," International Journal 65, no. 4 (2010): 913-930;
Andrew Crosby and Jeffrey Monaghan, "Settler Governmentality in Canada
and the Algonquins of Barriere Lake," Security Dialogue 43, no. 5
(2012): 421-438; Cameron Harrington and Emma Levcavalier, "The
Environment and Emancipation in Critical Security Studies: The Case of
the Canadian Arctic," Critical Studies on Security 2, no. 1 (2014):
105-119. 53 issues vital to Indigenous peoples -- such as environmental
change, linguistic and cultural preservation, natural resources, and
self-government -- are typically understood by policymakers and academic
audiences in the metropolitan south of Arctic states. In doing so, it
represents those issues that Indigenous peoples have articulated as most
pressing to their continued survival and wellbeing as security issues,
extending the theoretical logic of socially constructing in/security
beyond states and privileged national actors to include Indigenous
peoples. And in examining the ways in which Indigenous views of Arctic
security have not been successfully incorporated into the foreign and
security policies of circumpolar states, it indigenizes that discourse
and challenges it to account for the exclusion of Indigenous views. As
an academic study rooted in interpretivist, Western social scientific
methodology that also considers the views and practices of settler
states, this is at best a hybrid indigenist research project. However,
it aspires to contribute towards the decolonization of how Arctic
security is understood through the deliberate and focused incorporation
of those indigenous voices that have been silenced or subsumed. Perhaps
once the discourse has been decolonized, the policies and practices of
Arctic states will follow.

[In social movements throughout history, out of necessity, study and

They shed light on how the SNCC was primarily focused on civil rights,
particularly the right to vote, but also how the group discussed many
facets of the nation and the globe, including the movements happening in
nations far away. In other words, they studied racism and colonialism as
ways to inform their actions.

SNCC members read and discussed classic works by the first prime
minister and president of Ghana, the pan-Africanist Kwame Nkrumah, ﻿the
scholar and educator Carter G. Woodson, and Harriet Tubman, with a focus
on her practices of literally stealing back Black lives from slavery.
They researched the areas of the nation where particular obstacles made
it all but impossible for Black Americans to vote. For the members of
SNCC, the research led to empirical knowledge of how the daily
activities of Black Southerners included spaces of ease and, more
poignantly, barriers to voting and enacting their civil rights. For many
Black and Chicanx student groups in the 1960s, [political]{.underline}
[study]{.underline} [was]{.underline} first [about]{.underline}
**[achieving]{.underline}**

Claudia Jones consistently connected economic power structures across
nations, particularly in their impacts on Black peoples on the African

#### Indigenous knowledge production has historically been obscured and ignored in favor of Western intellectual authority which has resulted in false conclusions and epistemic violence 

**Martin, 2023**

(Peter R. - Assistant Professor in Cultural and Historical Geography @
University of Nottingham, "The 'deer-men' and the 'bowhead-men': The
colonial co-optation of Arctic Indigenous knowledge within the 'origins
of the Inuit' debates," Transactions of the Institute of British
Geographers, 49.1, 2024, article first published 22 May 2023,
https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/tran.12615)

This article has provided further evidence for the increasingly
recognised fact that Indigenous peoples contributed various types of
knowledge to the geographical discipline throughout its historical
development. [As ongoing research is making clear, [the vital role
played by Indigenous peoples in the]{.mark} historical [formation of
knowledge]{.mark} about our world [has]{.mark} regularly [been silenced,
obscured or ignored]{.mark}]{.underline} (Martin, 2020). Here, Nocum
provided Dall with various forms of geographical knowledge pertaining to
the Indigenous inhabitants of the Beringia region. Yet Dall avoided
giving the impression of an overreliance on this Indigenous informant
[[due to the risk it]{.mark}]{.underline} may [have [posed
to]{.mark}]{.underline} his own **[[intellectual
authority]{.underline}]{.mark}**. [Instead]{.underline} he invoked
[[racist arguments]{.underline}]{.mark} to [[diminish the credibility
of]{.underline}]{.mark} Nocum\'s [[claims]{.underline}]{.mark} and
called into question his intellectual authority while at the same time
increasing and bolstering his own status as a reliable and credible
scholar. Nocum\'s role in providing this information was thus carefully
manipulated within Dall\'s retelling of these events which has meant his
contributions have been omitted within historical retellings of the
'origin of the Inuit' debates. [This and similar processes have
therefore resulted in the skewed understandings]{.underline} of the
historical emergence of the geographical discipline as we understand it
today. [More importantly though, the article has identified [the racist
and imperialist logics]{.mark} bound up [in]{.mark} these historical
[debates]{.mark}]{.underline}. As both Smith (2021) and Livingstone
(2008) have made clear, [debates]{.underline} on human origins [have
regularly [result]{.mark}ed [in]{.mark} **[profoundly]{.mark}**
problematic and]{.underline} often [**[erroneous conclusions]{.mark}**
regarding human difference,]{.underline} and the debates pertaining
specifically to Inuit were no exception. [Yet [such
debates]{.mark}]{.underline} nonetheless [played a powerful role in
fomenting and reproducing problematic depictions of Arctic inhabitants,
depictions which were then disseminated across wider scholarship and
which in turn shaped public understandings]{.underline}. Dall\'s
writings undoubtedly played a part in [[reproduc]{.mark}ing]{.underline}
these troubling [[racialised imaginaries of Arctic
peoples]{.underline}]{.mark} and only now is his large body of work
beginning to receive due critical interrogation. As a final point, it is
vital to remember that the **[[colonial knowledge-making
practices]{.underline}]{.mark}** discussed here [[**cannot be
separated** from the]{.underline}]{.mark} various forms of **[[colonial
violence]{.underline}]{.mark}** that were to **[be [exerted upon the
Indigenous peoples of the Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline}** on both sides of

[[scholarly knowledge pertaining to]{.mark} Arctic [Indigenous

[[had direct]{.mark} and far-reaching [consequences for
how]{.mark}]{.underline} contemporary and future [[colonial regimes
enacted]{.mark} their political [power]{.mark}]{.underline} across the
Boreal regions (Stern & Stevenson, 2006). As is becoming ever more
evident, [the historical legacies of geographical, anthropological and
archaeological investigations in the Arctic are intimately intertwined
with histories of colonial dispossession, imperial control and
violence]{.underline} (Kulchyski, 1993; Rud, 2017; Tester & Kulchyski,
2011). The ways in which [[colonial knowledge]{.mark} regularly [led
to]{.mark} the [dispossession]{.mark} of land and territory, the [forced
relocation]{.mark} of entire communities, [and]{.mark} facilitation of
various forms of **[physical, psychological and epistemic harm toward

Leeuw, 2007; Harris, 2020; Loo, 2019; McLean, 2020). The insights
offered by this article seek to support ongoing scholarship that is

during this period (Barnd, 2017; Coulthard, 2014; Daigle, 2016; Deloria,
1997; de Leeuw, 2016; Smith, 2012; Louis, 2007). To conclude then, [it

[must]{.mark}]{.underline} also [[focus on the ways]{.mark} in which

#### The aff is a pre-requisite

**Holmberg et al, 2023**

(Aslak Holmberg - President of the Saami Council, Evie Morin - Masters
of Resource and Environmental Management from Simon Fraser University,
Anne Chahine -- PhD in Anthropology from Aarhus University, Nina Döring
-- PhD in Geography and the Environment from Oxford, Stephan Dudeck -
research fellow in Arctic Studies at the University of Tartu, Charleen
Fisher -- Professor of Alaska Native Studies and Rural Development at
University of Alaska Fairbanks, plus nine more authors, "Towards Arctic
Research Upholding Indigenous Peoples' Rights: Recommendations for ICARP
IV, the International Conference on Arctic Research Planning," 2023,
https://www.uaf.edu/rural/files/reports-and-publications/Policy%20brief%20%20ICARP%20IV%20-%20%20pages%20-%20web.pdf)

[[The Arctic is the ancestral home of diverse Indigenous Peoples, where
they have generated knowledge about]{.mark} their territories,
encompassing natural [environments]{.mark} and reciprocal relationships.
[This]{.mark} profound relationship of Arctic Indigenous Peoples with
their ecosystems [emphasises their crucial role in
research,]{.mark}]{.underline} which holds the potential to impact both
tangible and intangible elements of the natural world. [The history of
Arctic regions is one of [colonisation]{.mark} by domestic and foreign
governments whose policies and practises are still felt today. This,
[coupled with the influx of external researchers, brought unequal power
dynamics between knowledge systems and methodologies, causing harm to
local people and **undermining the quality of**]{.mark} **Arctic
[research]{.mark}**.]{.underline} Indigenous communities and trained
[[Indigenous researchers continue to lead research]{.mark} today.
[However]{.mark}]{.underline}, despite notable shifts in research
methodologies, [the enduring legacy of [colonialism continues to]{.mark}
cast a pervasive [influence]{.mark} over [the]{.mark} entire [research
continuum, from idea to]{.mark} conclusions, implementation, and
**[policy]{.mark}**]{.underline} outcomes. Introduction In this
document, we provide the ICARP IV process with concrete recommendations
for actions that can advance the parity and complementarity of
Traditional Knowledge, Indigenous Knowledge, and academic scientific
knowledge in order to inform the process and lead to better research
outcomes for all. However, this document cannot be used as a universally
applicable one-size-fits-all approach or as rigid, prescriptive
guidelines. The situations of Arctic Indigenous Peoples vary
significantly across the Circumpolar North due to diverse geographical,
environmental, and cultural factors, which influence their distinct
challenges, priorities, lifestyles, and interactions with the changing
Arctic environment. [[Dialogues]{.mark} and relationship-building [are
key to ensure ethical and effective research]{.mark} practices [that
genuinely serve]{.mark} to uplift the status, rights, and roles of
Arctic [Indigenous Peoples]{.mark}]{.underline}. In order to fulfil the
aforementioned commitment, [[Arctic research needs to be
decolonised]{.mark} and Indigenised]{.underline}. [[Decolonial research
translates to moving away from the typical institutional]{.mark} and
researcher-centric [systems]{.mark} towards]{.underline} genuine
[equality in]{.underline} planning and [decision-making on
research]{.underline} strategies, processes, and funding. The inclusion
of Indigenous rightsholders at eye-level in all decisionmaking, planning
and funding of Arctic research, on equal footing with the relevant
institutions at the nation-state level, advances the realisation of
Indigenous Peoples right to self-determination. Selfdetermination is the
right of Indigenous Peoples to independently guide research processes,
ensuring alignment with their values and needs, thereby enhancing the
relevance and impact of research outcomes. [Decolonising research is
crucial for Indigenous self-determination]{.underline} (see: Example 1).
Championing Indigenous Knowledge has also yielded ground-breaking
discoveries (see: Example 2), which would not have happened without
working with Indigenous Knowledge Holders. [Some research inquiries are
most aptly addressed by Indigenous communities themselves.]{.underline}
Overall, [[decolonial research]{.mark} approaches [lead to better
science]{.mark}, better knowledge, improved understanding, [and]{.mark}
better [decision-making]{.mark} results]{.underline}. In light of these
considerations, we have formulated a set of recommendations for the
ICARP IV process.

## Other 1AC Options

### Water Method

#### Water is a connection \[not sure\]

**Simpson '25** Simpson is a [Mississauga
Nishnaabeg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mississaugas) writer,
musician, and academic; known for her work with "Idle No More", and is a
faculty member at the [Dechinta Centre for Research and
Learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dechinta:_Centre_for_Research_and_Learning).
(Leanne Betasamosake, "Theory of Water: Nishnaabe Maps to the Time
Ahead", *Haymarket Books,* April 2025, Accessed via University of
Michigan Library System, DOA: 7/14/2025)//LFS---LMH

[[Colonialism]{.mark} has [forced]{.mark} [Indigenous people]{.mark}s
[to talk, think and organize around land in]{.mark} our
[resistance]{.mark} to ongoing dispossession. [But]{.mark} within
Nishnaabeg culture [there are also responsibilities for]{.mark} the
caretaking of [water]{.mark},]{.underline} and often these reside in the
realm of women or people who give birth. Often in our ceremonies, these
people will sing and pray to water, focusing on expressing our communal
love, dependency and gratitude for Nibi's life-giving qualities. [And
some have become activists. From 2003 to 2017, Elder Josephine Mandamin
led a movement of [water walkers]{.mark}]{.underline}---people who
walked around the Great Lakes, [[drawing attention to the health]{.mark}
and well-being [of]{.mark} the [water]{.mark}]{.underline}. The activist
Autumn Peltier from Wikwemikong has been fighting for access to clean
drinking water for a decade, since she was twelve years old. Mohawk
midwife Katsi Cook teaches us that [[water]{.mark} in our mothers [is
our first environment]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} the health of the [amniotic
fluid that surrounds us]{.mark} there [is]{.mark} directly [related to
the health of the aquifers and lakes]{.mark} that surround us in the
larger world.]{.underline} Meanwhile, Nishnaabeg fasting ceremonies,
which include spending time on the land in the spring and going for four
days and four nights without water, reinforce the idea that we cannot
exist very long without this element.

[[All genders and]{.mark} all forms of [life have a relationship with
water]{.mark}. Water [droplets]{.mark} in our breath [connect us
intimately]{.mark},]{.underline} as we learned during the pandemic.
[Nishnaabeg think of water [as the lifeblood of the earth]{.mark}. We
think of the [Great Lakes as]{.mark} internal
[organs]{.mark}]{.underline} that filter and clean water before sending
it along the Gchi-Ziibing, or the St. Lawrence River, to the Atlantic
Ocean. In this sense, the health of the Great Lakes is akin to the
health of our own kidneys and liver. [We rely upon coastal peoples to
take care of the water in the Arctic, Pacific and Atlantic Oceans. We
rely on northern peoples to take care of the permafrost, snow and sea
ice. We rely on Indigenous peoples in deserts and arid regions around
the globe to advise us of the great power that even a little bit of
water holds.]{.underline} We rely on mountain peoples to take care of
headwaters and glaciers. In my own practice, I rely on writers and
thinkers like Christina Sharpe and Alexis Pauline Gumbs to teach me what
Black feminists theorize about the Atlantic and the middle passage.

#### Ancestors work through sounds of rushing water -- wave sound asks us to listen and embody it

**Simpson '25** Simpson is a [Mississauga
Nishnaabeg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mississaugas) writer,
musician, and academic; known for her work with "Idle No More", and is a
faculty member at the [Dechinta Centre for Research and
Learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dechinta:_Centre_for_Research_and_Learning).
(Leanne Betasamosake, "Theory of Water: Nishnaabe Maps to the Time
Ahead", *Haymarket Books,* April 2025, Accessed via University of
Michigan Library System, DOA: 7/14/2025)//LFS---LMH

[I think of [our ancestors]{.mark}, who [spoke]{.mark} to their
ancestors [through the sound of rushing water]{.mark}. It's one of the
many reasons that hydro [dams]{.mark}, interpretive centres and [lift
locks are]{.mark} so [irritating]{.mark} to us: [they close off]{.mark}
channels of [communication]{.mark}. The sound of [rushing water is
a]{.mark} kind of [portal]{.mark} to another world.]{.underline} Places
where waves of energy altered human hearing were often where spiritual
beings lived or where certain significant events happened. [And the
sounds in these places, such as that of water moving, were
normalized.]{.underline} The idea that Belmore would have to stop us and
amplify the sounds so we could hear them would be startling to these
ancestors.

While the relevance of Ayum-ee-aawach Oomama-mowan still resonates
today, the work also gave birth to siblings in 2017, in a series called
Wave Sound. [[Revisiting]{.mark} [and inverting the]{.mark} concept of
the [megaphone]{.mark}, Belmore made four [sculptures,]{.mark} varying
in shape and form, [to amplify the sound of water and
shoreline]{.mark}]{.underline}. The sculptures were installed in Banff
National Park, Alberta, Gros Morne National Park in Newfoundland, in
Anishinaabe territory at Pukaskwa National Park in Ontario and on
reserve at Chimnissing Island in Georgian Bay, which is part of the
Beausoleil First Nation in Ontario. Three of the 170-pound conical
listening devices were made of aluminum casts, an aesthetic echo of who
the listeners were likely to be in the locations where these three were
installed: non-Indigenous peoples. For the sculpture on reserve land,
Belmore used copper, a material that echoes Anishinaabe history, story
and material use. [[Copper is]{.mark} said to be [the blood]{.mark} of
two groups of Manidoog, or spirits, Animikiig (Thunderbirds) and
Mishibizhiig (the Underwater Panthers), who engaged in an epic battle in
the Great Lakes region, spilling their blood on shorelines and rocky
outcrops. Copper [offers itself]{.mark} to the Anishinaabeg [by making
itself visible]{.mark} on the ground. It is [used to hold water in our
ceremonies, and represents space]{.mark}s [in]{.mark} [between]{.mark}
the [sky]{.mark}, [water]{.mark}s [and land]{.mark}.15]{.underline} To
make the sculptures, Belmore cast the rock in each site-specific
location, rock that was created out of thousands of years of wind, rain,
snow and ice, and then wrapped the castings around conical frames. The
forms were helicoptered into the sites so that visitors would happen
upon them while hiking. These were large-scale, temporary installations,
camouflaged in their surrounds, amplifying the unique sounds of each
place.16

Instead of inviting us to speak, [[Wave Sound asks us to practice
embodied listening]{.mark}---a deep listening and feeling using one's
entire being.]{.underline} [Wave Sound is [an invitation to
gather]{.mark} at the shore, [sit]{.mark} down [and use our bodies to
hear]{.mark} wind, rock, sand---maybe even mosquitoes and the durational
rhythm of waves.]{.underline} Iris Blake writes that Wave Sound gestures
towards listening and disability, with the conical devices functioning
as hearing aids.17 I think that it suggests how settlers under
colonialism have never, ever been able to hear the expansive web of
living things that we refer to as land, just as they have never heard
Indigenous peoples. When I encounter it, even in a gallery setting[[, I
don't look]{.mark} at Wave Sound---although I could for a long time,
because it is aesthetically stunning.]{.underline} [[Instead, I become
enmeshed]{.mark} in Wave Sound, [placing my body on the land to]{.mark}
quiet it, and to [listen]{.mark} [beside]{.mark} Nibi, to listen
[through]{.mark} Nibi, to listen [with]{.mark} Nibi---a being that
changes form from liquid to solid to gas, that travels the skyworld, the
underground, and inside and outside my body, an ancient formation of
molecules that has been the same since the beginning of this world. [A
fugitive that erodes and escapes containment,]{.mark} and one
[that]{.mark} [we]{.mark} simply [cannot live
without]{.mark}.]{.underline}

#### Love is a precursor and ethic to understanding and dismantling colonialism

**Simpson '25** Simpson is a [Mississauga
Nishnaabeg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mississaugas) writer,
musician, and academic; known for her work with "Idle No More", and is a

[[Loves]{.mark}. As I write those words in this spring moment, in the

the Jackson Creek trail, [I was thinking about [the child]{.mark} in me

It [was love. Not a fleeting]{.mark} romantic [notion]{.mark},
[but]{.mark} a deep sense of [care, hope, truth and kindness]{.mark}---a
loving any way, and all ways.]{.underline}

[[Before]{.mark} the idea of [the world]{.mark}, before the dreaming,
before the making, before the inspiration, the know-how, the
intercommunal creations, [there was care. There was
love.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[[This]{.mark} story [tells us that that love is the necessary precursor
to world building]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[I understand [love]{.mark} encoded in the word gzhwe [as being
unconditional;]{.mark} I understand love as [an organizing force, a
motion and an ethic of caring.]{.mark} I understand love [as]{.mark}
[communal]{.mark}---not bound up in the individual or with the
romantic.]{.underline} And of course, I understand it this way because
this is how Doug understood it. [As an ethic that is animated within our

that make up our world. [[It is not a structure]{.mark} [that negates

colonialism]{.mark}, responses that might include anger and resentment
and even violence. [Love in this form is a re-enactment,]{.mark}
[and]{.mark} like all such practices it can be wholeheartedly
manipulated and exploited under colonial rule[. It can be
conditioned,]{.mark} manipulated [and weaponized.]{.mark}]{.underline}

### Ice Version 

#### Rhetoric of exploitation and development of ice creates harmful policies that reproduce violent ideology and practice 

**Smith '25** (Jen Rose Smith is Assistant Professor of American Indian
Studies and Geography at the University of Washington; Ice Geographies:
The Colonial Politcs of Race and Indigeneity in the Arctic; Duke
University Press; May 2025; <https://doi.org/10.1215/9781478060758>,
DOA: 6/29/25)//LFS---LMH

OTHERWORLDLY ICE GEOGRAPHIES [Historically, [ice geographies]{.mark}
have been [consistently]{.mark} [described as "unearthly,]{.mark}"
"otherworldly," and "celestial" [by imperial
explorers]{.mark}]{.underline}.67 Explorers wrote of ice geographies as
spectral and supernatural spaces--- eerie, unfamiliar, and unlike
anything they had experienced in their more temperate nation-states.
Apart from aesthetic and affective renderings by those foreign to ice
geographies, polar spaces have been materially entwined with the cosmos.
[Through the discovery of comet dust in Antarctica to the very element
of water first coming to Earth in an icy form through comet landing,
[ice and outer space find themselves]{.mark} consistently
[linked]{.mark}]{.underline}. Ancient intellectuals and contemporary
thinkers look to the stars and the poles to make sense of the globe's
geography, of whiteness, and of philosophy and science. Before
nineteenth-century polar exploration, legends of mythic Hyperborea and
Arktikos as outrageous geographies wherein roamed beasts, monsters, and
strange human-like races were mainstays of Greek storytelling. [[Greeks
narrated]{.mark} the mythological geography of [Hyperborea as a secret
Eden]{.mark}, a circle of fertile, temperate climate located [in]{.mark}
the center of [a frozen wasteland]{.mark}. [Nazi ideology]{.mark} later
took up and [deployed]{.mark} not just [Hyperborea]{.mark} but [also saw
Antarctica as rich narrative ground for a white, severe
continent]{.mark}. Hyperborea specifically was chosen by the Third Reich
as a [pristine, fertile, and untouched]{.mark} landscape surrounded by a
blanket of whiteness and as the locale [from which the purity of the
Aryan race emerged]{.mark}.68 [The North Pole]{.mark}, [in addition to
being part and parcel of white-nationalist-masculinist desires]{.mark}
of its first discovery, [is bound to Europe's scientific
revolution]{.mark}.]{.underline} When understood as a celestial pole or
pole star, the North Pole can be read as one source for locating the
origins of a Western scientific sense of time and orientation in and
navigation through space. When one is not positioned in the high North
and navigating by the North Star, all other celestial bodies seem to
revolve around the North Pole's stationary spatial locatedness in the
sky. Geographical situatedness is key, for as Michael Bravo writes, some
Inuit living in the high North do not treat the North Star, or
Nuutuittuq, in the same way; because of the North Star's high elevation,
it is not a useful constellation for navigation.69 Some Inuit use
constellations or skymarks that are more conducive to their own
practical navigations. Today, [[ice geographies are utilized as
landscapes of simulation]{.mark} [for actual otherworldly
expeditions]{.mark} and excursions. [Ice is being hunted]{.mark} across
the solar system to evidence the potential for human survival on other
worlds and sought after by the wealthiest elite on the
planet.]{.underline} In the summer of 2021, many citizens of Earth
observed several rockets launch into space. From the United States,
three billionaires all fired rockets into the atmosphere. In 2023, an
ice-hunting rover was sent to the moon to obtain a more accurate
assessment of lunar ice amounts and "how much of it will be accessible
to humanity." 70 In 2021, nasa cut a tencent check to kick-start moon
mining technology, the first payment on a space resource contract to
collect lunar dust, volatiles, and water-ice. The reporting article on
nasa's lunar resource extraction support stated that the check "sets a
legal precedent for companies to go out and collect resources from the
lunar surface and make them basically useful for humanity."71 We also
see the logic of "usefulness for all humanity" unfold in the newest
version of the space race as filtered through capitalist logics. As
Bloomberg reports, "China, US Are Racing to Make Billions from Mining
the Moon's Minerals." 72 [And yet, those [industrialized nations]{.mark}
and individuals who are most reliant on fossil fuel extraction on planet
Earth [seek water-ice on the moon]{.mark}, and with every rocket launch
they expel up to three hundred tons of carbon dioxide into the upper
atmosphere where it can remain for years, quickening the pace of
[melting ice on the planet]{.mark} [we]{.mark} all currently
[inhabit]{.mark}.73 [These initiatives destroy ice on Earth while
seeking ice on earthly satellites. The figure of the hero never
dies.]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### Ice is dialectical at its core -- filled with contradictions that drive the impossibility for it to be coopted by colonial practices and that colonial practices will only cause certain death and demise because of ignorance of native understandings of cooperation with land

**Smith '25** (Jen Rose Smith is Assistant Professor of American Indian
Studies and Geography at the University of Washington; Ice Geographies:
The Colonial Politcs of Race and Indigeneity in the Arctic; Duke
University Press; May 2025; <https://doi.org/10.1215/9781478060758>,
DOA: 6/29/25)//LFS---LMH

[In a similar fashion with other agentic materialities across this
chapter, [Native peoples around the Arctic have]{.mark} long imagined
and [produced]{.mark} [knowledge]{.mark} [regarding their relationships
to the Arctic and to ice]{.mark} geographies. Fred [Bigjim]{.mark}, for
instance, from Sinrock and Nome, Alaska, writes in his poem "Ice
Floe,"]{.underline} Sitting here, waiting for a seal, I become
hypnotized By the ice floe To a point where I don't feel the cold, Or
care how hard the wind blows. I sit here being coy and bold. The ice
moves back and forth, Moaning, screeching, and screaming. It is as
though the ice itself is alive Lecturing me, Trying to tell me its
meaning. Telling me that I must work hard to survive. I watch the ice
move, Hiding and protecting Resources I need. The ice floe watches me,
It sounds like it's laughing. Underneath the ice floe, Sea creatures
feed I sit on top, Waiting. Sitting on the ice is my lifestyle, My
culture, My being. Overhead, watching me, is a crow. Ice---beautiful,
pure, Dangerous, and freezing. My life is so involved With the movement
Of the ice floe.56 Bigjim's poem [[chronicles an interaction between
hunter, ice, seal, and crow.]{.mark} [Ice has depth; it makes choices,
it sings, and it screams.]{.mark} All subjects of this poem are
watching, which offers the reader multiple perspective experiences. [The
hunter watches]{.mark} for the seal, [sea creatures]{.mark} feed and
[are attentive]{.mark} to what might be above the ice waiting for them,
the crow watches from on high, and [the ice watches and audibly
remarks]{.mark} on all that unfolds]{.underline}. The landscape of this
ice floe is dynamic and active. Each piece turns on the behavior and
expectation of the other(s). [A reader might recognize some of the more
common, [romanticized]{.mark} [characterizations of ice]{.mark} such [as
"beautiful, pure, / Dangerous, and freezing]{.mark}."]{.underline}
However, these lines in Bigjim's poem do not mete out the same meanings
as when delivered by a temporary visitor. [[Ice is not to be trifled
with; it is dangerous and requires]{.mark} an [attention]{.mark} and an
awareness that is ever present]{.underline}---that one must work hard to
survive. Warren Miaraq Jones discusses similar tenets in his essay "The
Philosophy of the Circumpolar North," wherein he writes, "[The arctic
and subarctic are unforgiving for those who don't understand them. [If
you don't understand and respect the environment it will take your
life]{.mark}. [Modern technology]{.mark} and life [delude]{.mark}
[people]{.mark} into a false sense of security.]{.underline} This land
will still kill you."57 This philosophy is not meant to dramatize the
severity or reinforce a hard-edged masculinity of ice geographies but is
one that proffers an orientation to landscape through a set of laws and
relationship that have been honed, refined, and upheld over time. Tanya
Tagaq writes, "The Land has no hierarchy. The Land has no manners; you
only obey and enjoy what is afforded to you by her greatness. Only logic
and great care ensure your survival. Only patterns of skills gifted by
our ancestors keep us living . . . we obey or we succumb."58 Bathsheba
Demuth's writing aligns in saying, "In the Arctic, such attention is not
an option but is necessary, and it yields appreciation for the
precarity, the contingency, of simply being."59 Jones writes in another
essay about this formulation as a particular kind of Yup'ik awareness,
drawing from Harold Napoleon's writing on yuuyaraq, or "the way of the
human being."60 Jones states, "[Our laws were known by all, and every
Yuk was responsible for holding themselves and others accountable to
yuuyaraq laws and structures. Yuuyaraq is the product of thousands of
years of wisdom from people living and learning the lessons of the
North]{.underline}."61 This Yup'ik sense of awareness is formulated in
co-constitution with the land and its particularities, making it
resonate with Bigjim's offering that an Inupiaq life is "so
involved / with the movement / of the ice floe."

#### The arctic is not white -- physically, ice is blue, deep, and rich; ethnically, the arctic is full of a diverse background of communities that are underrepresented -- recognizing the importance of color towards communities unlocks radical potential of understanding the arctic as a sight of revolution and beauty

**Smith '25** (Jen Rose Smith is Assistant Professor of American Indian
Studies and Geography at the University of Washington; Ice Geographies:
The Colonial Politcs of Race and Indigeneity in the Arctic; Duke
