#### CI: USFG = has no right to exist - topical affs should interrogate the USFG as an ongoing structure of invasion 

**Kashyap, 2020**

(Monika Batra, "U.S. Settler Colonialism, White Supremacy, and the
Racially Disparate Impacts of COVID-19," California Law Review, Nov.
2020,
https://www.californialawreview.org/settler-colonialism-white-supremacy-covid-19)

[[The U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [must be perceived and
analyzed]{.mark} [fundamentally as a settler colonial society created by
settlers who arrived with a presumption of]{.mark} sovereign
[entitlement and a]{.mark}n unshakeable [belief in their right to
establish a state over which they could exercise permanent and exclusive
control]{.mark}]{.underline}.\[1\]\[1\]See Natsu Taylor Saito, Tales of
Color and Colonialism: Racial Realism and Settler Colonial Theory, 10
Fla. A&M U. L. Rev. 1 (2014) (arguing that the United States must be
analyzed as a settler colonial society in order to accurately understand
the structural dynamics of race and racism in America)\.... This
"unshakeable belief" was bolstered and supported by White supremacist
logics in which European White men were understood as superior to all
other races and thus, by definition, had a right to possess and control
their land and labor.\[2\]\[2\]Shannon Speed, The Persistence of White
Supremacy: Indigenous Women Migrants and the Structures of Settler
Capitalism, 122 Am. Anthropology 77 (2020) (noting that the racial
logics underpinning U.S. settler colonialism's processes of Indigenous
dispossession, slavery, and immigrant exploitation were fundamentally
premised on White supremacy)\.... Unlike in "classic colonialism" or
"franchise colonialism" where the aim was to extract resources but where
the colonists did not intend to settle permanently, U.S. settlers
intended to permanently settle and form new ethnic and religious
sovereign communities on the newly acquired land.\[3\]\[3\]Jürgen
Osterhammel, Colonialism: A Theoretical Overview 11, 15 (2005)\.... In
order to do so, settlers first removed, dominated, destroyed, and
replaced Indigenous populations; the settlers then made the invaded
lands profitable by importing enslaved and exploitable
labor.\[4\]\[4\]Saito, supra note 1, at 7. **[[A set]{.mark}tler
[col]{.mark}onialism [framework recognizes]{.mark} that [the
U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [is a present-day settler colonial
society whose laws, institutions, and systems of governance continue to
enact]{.mark} an [ongoing]{.mark} "structure of
[invasion]{.mark}]{.underline}**" that persists to this
day.\[5\]\[5\]Patrick Wolfe, Settler Colonialism and the Elimination of
the Native, 8 J. Genocide Res. 388, 390 (2006) ("Settler colonizers come
to stay: invasion is a structure not an event."); Lorenzo Veracini, The
Settler Colonial Present 9 (2015) ("\[S\]ettler colonialism forever
proclaims its passing, but it never goes away.")\.... Scholars across
multiple disciplines have turned towards using a settler colonialism
framework in their analyses to broaden understandings of how systems of
subordination are structured in the United States.\[6\]\[6\]See, e.g.,
Saito, supra note 1; Evelyn Nanako Glenn, Settler Colonialism as
Structure: A Framework for Comparative Studies of U.S. Race and Gender
Formation, 1 Soc. Race & Ethnicity 52 (2015) (using a settler
colonialism framework to explain the structural dynamics of race and
gender formation in the United States); Kelly Lytle Hernández, City of
Inmates: Conquest, Rebellion, and the Rise of Human Caging in Los
Angeles, 1771-- 1965 (2017) (using a settler colonialism framework to
explain the rise of mass incarceration in the United States);
Speed, supra note 2 (using a settler colonialism framework to explore
how White supremacy is structured into U.S. institutions and everyday
social relations)\.... [A framework of settler colonialism understands
that [the]{.mark}]{.underline} three [[foundational process]{.mark}es
up[on]{.mark} [which the]{.mark} United [State]{.mark}s [was
built]{.mark}---[Indigenous elimination, anti-Black racism, and
immigrant exploitation---]{.mark}are ongoing processes that]{.underline}
continue to [[shape present-day]{.mark} systemic
[inequities]{.mark}]{.underline}[.\[]{.mark}7\]\[7\]See generally
Saito, supra note 1; Glenn, supra note 6. In other words, **[a settler
colonialism framework acknowledges the endurance of]{.underline}** three
ongoing "strategies of **[colonization]{.underline}**" that continue to
maintain settler colonialism's structure of invasion: 1) strategies
of elimination targeting Indigenous peoples; 2) strategies of
subjugation targeting Black people (anti-Black racism); and 3)
strategies of exploitation and exclusion targeting immigrants of
color.\[8\]

### AT: Fairness

#### [UNCRITICAL EQUITY]{.underline}. It's [violent]{.underline}\-\--that's more [unfair]{.underline}. If unquestioned [procedural]{.underline} fairness is [intrinsically]{.underline} [good]{.underline}, go play [chess]{.underline}!

**Oliver, 23**---Department of Philosophy, Vanderbilt University (Kelly,
"The Limits and Dangers of Risk-Benefit Analysis: From the Refugee
Crisis to the Coronavirus Pandemic," *The Vulnerability of the Human
World: Well-being, Health, Technology and the Environment*, Chapter 2,
pp. 13--27, SpringerLink, dml)

Justice requires continually renegotiating the relationship between
ethics and politics, singular and universal, precisely because there is
always something in excess, something beyond recognition.
[This]{.underline} remainder [is the **[ethical imperative]{.mark}**
that should **keep us awake at night**]{.underline}. [Rather than lead
to **inquietude** or **apathy**]{.underline}, the excess beyond our
[recognition keeps us **actively searching** for better ways to approach
justice **without** resting on]{.underline} moral rules or [risk-benefit
calculations]{.underline} or self-righteous principles. [The
remainder]{.underline} beyond calculation, beyond recognition,
[[demands]{.mark} that [we]{.mark} continue to **[question]{.mark}**
our]{.underline} own principles and **[policies]{.underline}**, [that we
continue to examine our concepts of]{.underline} justice and
**[[fairness]{.underline}]{.mark}** and equality.

An [ethical politics]{.underline} is one in which justice demands the
hyper-vigilance self-critical reflection on where any response---ethical
or political---necessarily falls short. It is an ongoing task that
[**cannot end** with risk-benefit analysis]{.underline}. [Despite the
**ethical insomnia** and **political vertigo** caused by this
interminable self-questioning]{.underline}, [we must
**act**]{.underline}---[even **leap**]{.underline}. Although ultimately
the gap between singularity and universality is impassible, [the
impossibility of achieving justice is **no excuse** for not **attempting
it every single day**]{.underline}.

This tension between politics (which addresses itself to what can be
seen, and to the universal, what can be shared), and ethics (which
addressed itself to what cannot be seen, and to the singular, what
cannot be shared) is the knot at the center of justice.

[Justice is **antithetical** to utilitarian ethics insofar as
individuals are **not fungible units** that can be
exchanged]{.underline}. [Utilitarianism relies on the **calculability**
of]{.underline} pain, suffering, **[survival]{.underline}**, [or the
quality of life]{.underline} itself. And the [utilitarian calculus
relies on determining **thresholds** that separate individuals into
groups]{.underline}, whether it is those who are members of the moral
community or the polis, and those who are not (for example animals), or
those [whose lives are **worth saving** and those whose lives are
**not**]{.underline}.

At some point, however, [this type of line-drawing **inevitably**
becomes either **political** or **arbitrary**]{.underline}. Take debates
over the basis for membership in the moral community. What are the
criteria for membership? Intelligence, language, reason, sentience, the
ability to suffer? And [whatever we decide should be the
criteria]{.underline}, [where do we **draw the line**?]{.underline} For
example, what counts as a language? Does the bees signal dance count?
Or, as some argue, is it merely a code and not a language? What counts
as intelligence? Peter Singer argues that great apes should be extended
rights or admitted to the moral community based on their intelligence,
which is greater than severely mentally disabled persons (see Singer,
1999; McMahan, 2009; Anstötz, 1993).Footnote 11 Some disability scholars
such as Eva Kittay have argued against this comparison, insisting that
human beings should be accorded special status regardless of their
mental abilities (Kittay, 1998a, b, 2009).Footnote 12 The controversy
over Singer's argument demonstrates how [utilitarian line-drawing is
always a **fraught endeavor** that ultimately rests on an **arbitrary
cut off point**]{.underline}.

For example, in Animal Liberation, Singer draws the line between calms
and shellfish, who supposedly cannot feel pain (Singer, 1990,
173--4).Footnote 13 But what if we learn that shellfish can feel pain?
In all these utilitarian endeavors, the liminal cases are unsettling
because they reveal the arbitrariness of borders and the inadequacy of
general classifications. This type of line-drawing or threshold not only
ignores the singularity of each individual, but also poses an arbitrary
or political dividing line as a natural border. The same is true of
national borders used to keep migrants out.

We see something similar in covid triage medicine where triage protocols
disadvantage whole classes of people. Old people, disabled people,
cancer survivors. The coronavirus lays bare the fantasy of calculable
criteria as an ethical way of life. Covid reveals gaping inequities in
the length and quality of life caused by social and economic
"pre-existing conditions." Celebrities and politicians from Madonna to
Andrew Cuomo called the coronavirus the "great equalizer." In March
2020, Cuomo tweeted "This virus is the great equalizer." In reality,
covid is the great unequalizer... Footnote 14

The pandemic forces doctors and nurses to make impossible decisions
about who is given the chance to live and who will die. But these
decisions exist in the context of pre-existing conditions of poverty,
racism, and unequal access to health care that skew any attempt at fair
or reasonable calculation. In a fair and just world, there would be
adequate resources available to everyone. Tragically, the real world is
not so fair or just. And [risk-benefit analysis and calculative
reasoning become the justification for **allowing some to
die**]{.underline}. [[When]{.mark} those [calculations pose as]{.mark}
**justice** or **[fair]{.mark}ness**]{.underline}, [and when they
[allow]{.mark} us to feel **[certain]{.mark}**]{.underline}, even
righteous, [in our **[decisions]{.mark}**]{.underline}, then [they risk
[a **slippery slope**]{.mark} that leads us to a place where **any** and
**all [life]{.mark}** can [become **disposable**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

#### Cruel Optimism DA

**WildEarth Guardians, 2018**

(WildEarth Guardians protects and restores the wildlife, wild places,

Santa Fe, N.M. --- **[Over 200 clean]{.underline}** air and **[water
advocates rallied in front of the]{.underline}** Bureau of Land

Santa Fe today **[to protest the]{.underline}** December 5th and 6th
**[online auction of]{.underline}** over
**[[89,000](https://eplanning.blm.gov/epl-front-office/projects/nepa/116415/160176/195838/DEC_2018_FINAL_SALE_NOTICE_107_PARCELS_508_.pdf)
acres of public and ancestral tribal lands]{.underline}**, including
over 44,000 acres in the Greater Chaco region, and over 40,000 near
Carlsbad Caverns National Park. **[[Tribal
leaders]{.underline}]{.mark}**, environmental groups, and advocates
**[[express]{.mark}ed growing [outrage at BLM's]{.mark} chronic
disengagement with the public]{.underline}**, **[its [lack of
meaningful]{.mark} tribal [consultation]{.mark}, [and its failure to
consider the]{.mark} public health and environmental [impacts of selling
off]{.mark} federal and [tribal lands for
fracking.]{.mark}]{.underline}** ["**[Meeting with BLM is like talking
to a wall]{.underline}**]{.mark}**[, and we have been hitting the wall
for a long time." said Samuel Sage]{.underline}**, Community Services
Coordinator for Counselor Chapter, **[Navajo Nation. "[For the BLM, it's
all about greed]{.mark}, but all we want is]{.underline}** clean air and
**[water for our kids and grandchildren]{.underline}**. They call us
'activists' when they should be calling oil and gas industry proposals
'terrorist.'" **[[Despite]{.underline}]{.mark}** receiving a
record-breaking 10,000 **[[protest]{.underline}]{.mark}** comments,
**[[the BLM is moving forward]{.mark} with the controversial
[leases]{.mark}]{.underline}** based on outdated Resource Management
Plans (RMPs) for the Farmington, Rio Puerco, Carlsbad, and Roswell Field
Offices, all of which were written before new fracking methods were
feasible and **[[without]{.underline}]{.mark}** meaningful tribal
consultation **[or [consent from Navajo Nation and
Pueblos]{.mark}]{.underline}** who consider the Greater Chaco region
sacred. "**[We have fought to have our Pueblo tribal leaders at the
table]{.underline}**, to have clean air and soil for the Dine
communities, **[and we have gone to D.C. to talk]{.underline}** about
our concerns as indigenous people. **[Yet, [BLM continues to lie]{.mark}
and neglect their responsibilities]{.underline}**." said Julia Bernal,
Co-Director of Pueblo Action Alliance. "That BLM has a mandated 'Trust
Relationship' with Tribal Nations and we must still demand to be
consulted is blatant disrespect. Maybe they think we'll leave, but we're
not going anywhere. Public lands are ancestral lands and we are the
land." Although BLM state headquarters were shuttered today due to a
federal day of mourning for former President George H. W. Bush, Greater
Chaco advocates proceeded with their planned rally to remind agency
officials of their
[promise](https://eplanning.blm.gov/epl-front-office/eplanning/planAndProjectSite.do?methodName=dispatchToPatternPage&currentPageId=98894)
to Tribes and the American public [to develop a new plan to protect
Greater
Chaco](https://climatewest.org/2017/06/22/new-hope-for-greater-chaco-or-more-broken-promises-to-come/).
Advocates wrote personalized messages to the BLM on a large banner that
read "Not Your Sacrifice Zone: We Demand Justice", and taped the banner
to the front wall of BLM headquarters. "Sacrifice Zone" is a label that
has stuck to the Four Corners region since the early 1970s, when the
Nixon Administration began prioritizing energy extraction policies, at
great cost to the communities, land, and water in the Southwest. With
their banner, advocates refused for New Mexico to remain a national
energy sacrifice zone, and demanded instead that the BLM engage in
meaningful tribal and public consultation, and ensure protections for
the environment and public health. Earlier this year, Interior Secretary
Zinke deferred the March lease sale of 4,434 acres [calling for more
cultural consultation admitting the Greater Chaco region needs more
cultural
study](https://www.blm.gov/press-release/blm-defers-oil-and-gas-lease-sale-parcels-new-mexico).
To date, [the BLM has yet to survey over 5,400 cultural resources in the
region]{.underline}. [Instead, the [BLM has rolled back opportunities
for the public to weigh in on oil and gas leasing, shortening "protest"
or appeal periods from 30 days to just 10
days](https://www.eenews.net/stories/1060072713) and]{.underline}
[[refusing]{.underline}](https://climatewest.org/2018/10/23/u-s-bureau-of-land-management-shutting-door-on-americans-auctioning-away-new-mexico-public-lands/)
[to hold any public hearings, even near impacted
communities**.**]{.underline} Advocate groups [currently have a lawsuit
asserting that drilling without a plan is
illegal](https://www.eenews.net/energywire/stories/1060084765/search?keyword=Chaco).
The [Navajo Nation and All Pueblo Council of
Governors](http://www.sanjuancitizens.org/oil-and-gas/navajo-nation-pueblo-council-join-forces-protect-greater-chaco/),
[National Congress of American
Indians](http://www.ncai.org/resources/resolutions/to-support-moratorium-on-leasing-and-permitting-in-greater-chaco-region),
[15 Navajo Chapter
Houses](https://www.frackoffchaco.org/statements-of-support), the [New
Mexico
Legislature](https://www.nmlegis.gov/Legislation/Legislation?chamber=H&legType=JM&legNo=5&year=17&AspxAutoDetectCookieSupport=1),
more than 100 organizations, and over 400,000 public citizens in total
have requested a moratorium on drilling until the BLM updates its
Resource Management Plans. The vast majority of public lands in the San
Juan Basin of Greater Chaco and in the Permian Basin around Greater
Carlsbad Caverns are already leased for oil and gas development, and
**[residents]{.underline}** nearby **[have been dealing with the impacts
of resource extraction for decades]{.underline}**. Today's rally sends
the message to officials that advocates refuse to relent until justice
is upheld. "Despite the Administration's recent climate report that
shows federal fossil fuels account for one quarter of U.S. greenhouse
gas emissions, this December BLM is selling off close to two million
acres of western public and tribal lands for oil and gas, and the New
Mexico agency is the most egregious offender of public process, offering
up nearly 100% of Greater Chaco and Greater Carlsbad for sacrifice,"
said Rebecca Sobel, Climate and Energy Senior Campaigner for WildEarth
Guardians. "It's clear Trump's 'Energy Dominance' agenda will stop at
nothing to sacrifice every inch of public land for private profit, but
today's event shows that the people are also relentless in our
commitment to rise up and demand justice for Greater Chaco and beyond."
A map of the oil and gas leases is available
[here](https://arcg.is/09yrf4).

The theory of Nibi asks us to think on a scale that is outside the
present moment and our own immediate needs. I'm thinking about Nibi as
theory here because Nibi offers us an invitation to learn from its
embodied practice, a practice of cycling that is global, is permeable
and brings about a continuous rebirth on our planet. [Nibi asks us to
[ground ourselves intimately in land and place]{.mark}, and relate that

create more possibilities. Water and Nibi are working within
mino-bimaadiziwin to propel a continuous, global rebirth of life.8

[[Nibi]{.underline}]{.mark}, like all Nishnaabeg theories, [[is]{.mark}

multiplicity, adaptation, iteration and decentralization]{.mark}, as
forces that create the conditions for life to emerge and
continue.]{.underline} Nibi is inside us, and it connects us to all
forms of life on the planet. Nibi matters.

#### Only the perm solves -- the harms of capitalism and colonialism are intertwined 

**Simpson '25** Simpson is a [Mississauga

There are now forty-two locks covering 386 kilometres of waterways in
the Trent-Severn system, giving Parks Canada complete control over the
water level throughout the areas affected.

[And the [impact has been devastating]{.mark}]{.underline} on Kina Gchi

### Perm -- Mutual Aid Alt

#### Mutual aid and life behind the barricades.

**Simpson '25** Simpson is a [Mississauga
Nishnaabeg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mississaugas) writer,
musician, and academic; known for her work with "Idle No More", and is a
faculty member at the [Dechinta Centre for Research and
Learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dechinta:_Centre_for_Research_and_Learning).
(Leanne Betasamosake, "Theory of Water: Nishnaabe Maps to the Time
Ahead", *Haymarket Books,* April 2025, Accessed via University of
Michigan Library System, DOA: 7/14/2025)//LFS---LMH

[[Mutual aid is a]{.mark} foundational [world-building process]{.mark}
of Nishinaabeg life]{.underline}, and it is easy to find examples of how
it manifests in our daily practices. An infant might first encounter
mutual aid through breastfeeding, the nurturing of attachment between
parent and child through the sharing of sustenance, connection and
warmth. A toddler, marching around, expressing in a true, unfiltered way
every emotion they might be feeling, brings humour and insight and
connection to the family. A teenager engaged in the fast life, pushing
boundaries, swimming in consequences, teaches us about different sorts
of freedom. [In adulthood, [storytellers become conduits of
knowledge]{.mark} as they share narratives---vessels of imagination,
thought, collaboration and co-creation.]{.underline}109 [And [ceremonies
enact healing, peace and intimacy]{.mark}. Hunters gift meat and fish;
harvesters gift rice, maple sugar, berries and medicines; grandparents
gift child care; Elders gift presence and experience. Artists gift
possibilities beyond our current structures. Singers and drummers gift
prayers.]{.underline}

[I think of mutual aid when [I recall Mohawk women]{.mark} gathering
around kitchen tables, [organizing to stop golf]{.mark} course
[expansions]{.mark}, condominium [development]{.mark}, enclosures around
nature areas. Or [when]{.mark} I remember the [women of Grassy
Narrows]{.mark} organizing to [protect]{.mark} their [lands and
bodies]{.mark} [from]{.mark} clear-cutting and toxic
[contamination]{.mark}. Or [Two-Spirit, queer and trans]{.mark} young
[people]{.mark} researching, writing and [distributing]{.mark} [their
work]{.mark} in You Are Made of Medicine: A Mental Health Peer Support
Manual for Indigiqueer, Two-Spirit, LGBTQ+, and Gender Non-conforming
Indigenous Youth.110 Or the [Braided Warriors, organizing a temporary
blockade]{.mark} of Indigenous youth in March 2020 [in unceded
territories]{.mark}]{.underline} of the Səl̓ílwətaʔ (Tsleil-Waututh),
Sḵwx̱wú7mesh (Squamish) and xʷməθkʷəy̓əm (Musqueam) nations, [[and]{.mark}
[demand]{.mark}ing the [release]{.mark} of an Elder jailed for
participation in protests [against]{.mark} [the]{.mark} Trans Mountain
[pipeline]{.mark}]{.underline} in Burnaby, BC.111 [Or the Tiny House
Warriors protecting Secwépemc lands]{.underline} from the same pipeline.
[Or the Unist'ot'en camp. #LandBack and 1492 Land Back Lane. And I could
go on, recalling and naming blockades as generative refusals of the
state]{.underline}---generative precisely because, behind the
barricades, collectives are rebuilding their own versions of
Gizhewaadiziwin. They are building worlds outside the ones we have
inherited.112 I'm thinking too, of the student encampments for Gaza that
blossomed in the spring of 2024, and the communities that supported them
in myriad ways, from decision-making to cooking to child care to
teach-ins to a solidarity that centred the voices of Gazans. I'm
thinking of the slogan "[[we keep us safe,"]{.underline}]{.mark} and
abolitionist practices put into practice, because of course university
administrations and the police were threatening violence.

In other words: [[behind]{.mark} the [barricades]{.mark},]{.underline}
whether those blockades are enacted on Anishinaabeg land at Grassy
Narrows, Dakota land at Standing Rock, at the port of Vancouver or at
Unist'ot'en, [[blockades]{.mark} [are]{.mark} rich [sites of]{.mark}
Indigenous [life]{.mark}.]{.underline}

In the spaces behind the barricades, [you'll find [parents]{.mark} with
[children]{.mark}. You'll find [Elders]{.mark}. You'll encounter
[ceremony]{.mark}, sacred [fires]{.mark} and [language]{.mark} learning.
[Art]{.mark} making. [Singing]{.mark}. [Drumming]{.mark}.
[Storytelling]{.mark}.]{.underline}

You'll find an ethic of care and mutual aid as harvesters and cooks
engage in a bush economy to feed the front lines alongside spiritual
leaders, nurses and medics taking care of the people. [You will notice
[a mobilized network]{.mark} of support and solidarity extending well
[beyond]{.mark} the barricades.]{.underline}

[You will witness the re-emergence of [political leaders]{.mark}, not
through a band council election but [through]{.mark} Indigenous
practices of deep [relationality]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[You will enter a collective [embodiment]{.mark} [of]{.mark} Indigenous

[engagement in these rich sites of knowledge]{.mark}
[production]{.mark}, and you'll see Indigenous anti-colonial [theory

practice.]{.underline}

In spite of dominant colonialism---in the face of it---you will witness
a radically different political existence and ethical orientation. This
existence, and this orientation, springs from a different premise than
the politics and economy of extraction. [The premise is to live with the
purpose of generating continual life]{.underline}.113

#### Solidarity in the rev.

**Garnham '21** Marxist-Leninist Activist (Sarah, "The failure of
identity politics: A Marxist analysis", *Marxist Left Review,* August
20, 2021,
<https://marxistleftreview.org/articles/the-failure-of-identity-politics-a-marxist-analysis/>,
DOA: 7/15/2025)//LFS---LMH

Solidarity and Revolution

Solidarity is the enduring catch-cry of all serious struggles against
oppression. Solidarity is often misunderstood today as a term that
refers to symbolic gestures of support or as interchangeable with the
concept of allyship, which is about passive support and assumes a
fundamental opposition of interests between identity groups. It is
neither of these things. It is fundamentally about power not symbolism,
and it is based on an understanding of the unity of interests between
different layers of society, embodied most importantly in the working
class.

Solidarity is firstly the process linking together struggles in order to
make each more powerful. This is a necessity because of the system that
we are up against, and it is especially important for groups that are
the most marginalised. [It is impossible for example for Aboriginal
people, trans people, or refugees to win liberation without significant
support from other social layers. They are simply too small and socially
peripheral to confront the ruling class and its state. It is necessary
for social movements that aim to achieve meaningful victories to win
over significant minorities of the population, if not majorities, to
their cause.]{.underline}

This is not to say that within particular movements the leadership and
input of those directly affected by oppression is not crucial. In fact,
precisely because of these experiences, they will often be the most
motivated to initiate struggle and are uniquely placed to inspire others
to join the movement. But it is not sufficient to then call on others to
gather around like a cheer squad. Building solidarity is not based upon
moral chastisement, nor on sympathetic paternalism. It is based
fundamentally on the recognition of shared interests and a collective,
democratic struggle.

[One of the main ways that a common interest between oppressed groups is
established is by recognising the common oppressor. Even without a
thoroughgoing analysis of capitalism, this often becomes clear to
people, especially in the course of struggle. Different groups are
oppressed by the same governments, scapegoated by the same media
outlets, attacked by the same police. As well as having a shared
experience of oppression at the hands of these forces, when our side
deals a blow to them on one front, it is a victory for all of us. For
instance, forcing a government into backing down on one regressive
policy can often have the effect of making them less willing to go after
other groups, as well as giving the broad left more confidence. And
because there is a tendency for struggle to break down the hold of
bourgeois ideology, the more that people engage with struggles not just
against their own oppression, but also against that directed at others,
the more that the ideas that can divide us are undermined.]{.underline}

The movement that the term solidarity is most closely associated with is
that of the working class. There are a few reasons for this. Firstly, it
is the working class that, of all oppressed groups, has the most social
power. In fact it has a social power that is unique across the entire
span of human history, the power to stop the flow of profits at the
point of production. This economic strength gives working-class
demonstrations of solidarity serious muscle. On multiple occasions the
deployment of this power has played a crucial role in struggles against
oppression, for example winning equal pay and land rights in Australia.
[Today there is a lack of awareness of this important aspect of
solidarity, both because identity politics has pushed people's
conception of struggles against oppression into the realms of culture
and symbols, and because the workers' movement is weak. This in turn
makes all of our other struggles weaker. Workers still make up the
majority of most demonstrations today, but usually as individuals,
rather than as an organised force prepared to take strike action or
initiate industrial bans on behalf of the movement. It is undoubtedly
the case that such actions would have substantially strengthened the
recent BLM protests, the climate strikes, and every other social
movement.]{.underline}

[The other reason that the working class generated the solidarity
principle is because it is a collective class. Workers have no power as
individuals; they can only threaten production collectively. This is
true within workplaces, but the collectivity of the working class goes
beyond this. Under capitalism, production is divided into millions of
processes carried out across millions of workplaces. The more that
workers can link up with struggles at other workplaces, the more power
they wield.]{.underline}

A couple of important things flow from this. One is that workers have an
interest in extending solidarity to other groups of workers, and in
breaking down sectionalism and the appearance of competition between
different sections of the working class. Another is that workers have an
interest in overcoming all forms of oppression. The working class
consists of all other oppressed groups, but also oppression sows
ideological and political divisions which are a barrier to collective
struggle.

[For workers to get to a point where they have enough power to challenge
the ruling class for power, thus overcoming their oppression, there
needs to be a high level of democratic coordination across many sections
of the class. For this to happen, workers need to be conscious of the
oppressive and divisive tactics of the ruling class, and to have a
strong sense of common purpose with groups of workers from many
different identity backgrounds. In this sense, conscious political
opposition to oppression is not distinct from or an optional add-on to
class consciousness. High levels of class consciousness -- meaning an
ability for workers to understand their collective class interests and
objectives -- necessarily implies opposition to oppression. This is not
to say that workers come to this consciousness automatically. Struggle,
and even more so, revolution, play a big part in shifting views. Also
crucial is political intervention and argument.]{.underline}

[More than just challenging oppression throughout the course of struggle
and revolution, workers represent the only force that can eradicate
oppression altogether]{.underline}. A socialist society ruled by the
working class will necessarily be run democratically and without
arbitrary forms of discrimination. This comes back again to the nature
of the working class in relation to production. It has no ability to
exploit any other layer because it does all of the work. And it is a
majority class that is only powerful as a collective. Therefore under
workers' rule there is no material basis for arbitrary divisions or
discrimination. Without these material underpinnings, there is no reason
for cultural norms and backward ideas that spring from inequality to
continue. After a period of development under socialism, oppression in
all of its forms will be eradicated. [Where capitalism cannot survive
without oppression, socialism cannot survive with it. The working class
is the only force capable of building such a society and in this way it
represents the only universalising social agent, capable of delivering
its own emancipation and, at the same time, liberation for all of
humanity.]{.underline}

### AT: Elite Capture

#### Elite capture is a result of colonialism, not something a "plan" can avoid

**Simpson '25** Simpson is a [Mississauga
Nishnaabeg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mississaugas) writer,
musician, and academic; known for her work with "Idle No More", and is a
faculty member at the [Dechinta Centre for Research and
Learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dechinta:_Centre_for_Research_and_Learning).
(Leanne Betasamosake, "Theory of Water: Nishnaabe Maps to the Time
Ahead", *Haymarket Books,* April 2025, Accessed via University of
Michigan Library System, DOA: 7/14/2025)//LFS---LMH

The never-ending flow of the [global [water cycle]{.mark} reciprocally
[nourishes]{.mark} [life]{.mark} on the planet [and]{.mark} teaches us
about [accountability]{.mark}.]{.underline} When part of the cycle is
disrupted, modes of reciprocity are disrupted, and the everyday cycle of
accountability is disturbed.

[[What happens when the]{.mark} accountability [system is
interrupted]{.mark}]{.underline}? Recently, I've been rereading and
thinking with Olúfẹ́mi O. Táíwò's [[Elite Capture]{.underline}]{.mark}:
How the Powerful Took Over Identity Politics (and Everything Else). In
my reading this time, I'm thinking not so much about identity politics
but about what's in parentheses---the "everything else." Táíwò defines
"elite capture" as "a concept that originated in developing countries to
describe the way socially advantaged people tend to gain control over
financial benefits, specifically foreign aid, meant for others." And the
concept, he writes, "[[has also been applied]{.mark} more generally
[to]{.mark} [describe]{.mark} how [political projects]{.mark} can be
hijacked [in principle or effect]{.mark} by the well positioned and
resourced.]{.underline}121

In response to this[, I'm thinking about how [Indigenous]{.mark}
[political projects are hijacked]{.mark} in principle and in effect
[by]{.mark} the [Canad]{.mark}i[a]{.mark}n state,]{.underline} and how
over time Indigenous political projects become more responsive to the
ruling class, or elites, or the state than they are to Indigenous
communities "(I'm also thinking I'm old---since I used to understand
this concept not as "elite capture" but as hegemony122---even as I
admire the brilliance, clarity and power of Táíwò's thinking.)

From an Indigenous perspective---and certainly from within the Michi
Saagiig Nishnaabeg perspective, which [I would characterize as radically
egalitarian---"[elite capture]{.mark}" [arrived through
colonialism]{.mark}. It is a [tool]{.mark} of the system [of
colonialism]{.mark}, one that became [prominent]{.mark} [after]{.mark}
the Indian Act [amendments]{.mark} of 1951, [when]{.mark} Indigenous
[peoples started to organize]{.mark} in ways that made our struggles
more visible to the outside. For one thing, we were then allowed to
gather our resources, and hire lawyers, and organize in ways that had
not been available to us in the past.]{.underline} Over the past forty
years in Canada, the idea of Indigenous self-determination has yielded
an unprecedented degree of recognition for Aboriginal and Treaty Rights
within the apparatus of the state, including through the Constitution
Act of 1982. [At the same time, anti-imperialist [traditions have been
used to refute]{.mark} [the]{.mark} idea that the [relationship]{.mark}
between Indigenous peoples and the state [can be transformed]{.mark}
[by]{.mark} the [politics of recognition]{.mark}.]{.underline} As Glen
Coulthard argues, drawing on Frantz Fanon, the terms of recognition in
Canada are determined solely by the state, and while the politics of
recognition have replaced the state's more overt policy of assimilation,
the state is still trying to extinguish and eliminate Indigenous Title
and power, and to control land and capital.123" "[[But now]{.mark} it
strikes me, reading Táíwò, that Indigenous political [projects]{.mark},
[framed]{.mark} [in]{.mark} the politics of [recognition]{.mark}, [have
set up our rebellions and revolts for elite
capture.]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### Elite capture is inevitable but participating in state-based politics makes extraction inevitable 

**Simpson '25** Simpson is a [Mississauga

[Twenty years of the Slant Lake [blockade]{.mark} [demonstrates]{.mark}

#### **Racism w/in the movement**

**Saba '80** Long-time communist activist (Paul, "Racism in the
Communist Movement", December 1980,
<https://www.marxists.org/history/erol/ncm-6/oc-racism/racism-ocic/intro.htm>,
DOA: 7/19/2025)//LFS---LMH

A Closer Look at White Chauvinist Ideology and the Struggle Against it
Among Communists

[The Philadelphia Workers Organizing Committee (PWOC) paper, "[Racism in
the PWOC]{.mark}," gives a [good]{.mark} [description of white
chauvinist ideology]{.mark} (i.e., the views about national minorities),
its historical [development]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} the various
[components]{.mark} of white chauvinist ideology.]{.underline} Comrades
should refer to that paper for a background on the historical
development and various aspects of white chauvinist ideology. What we
will discuss here is the forms of white chauvinism -- overt racism and
racist paternalism -- and petty-bourgeois chauvinism and its interaction
with white chauvinism. We will examine their impact on the communist
movement.

The PWOC paper discusses the two poles of racist ideology -- [overt
racism and [racist paternalism]{.mark}]{.underline} -- and how they are
each [expressions of the same underlying assumptions about national
minorities]{.underline}. Overt racism is described as open race hatred
and naked force, while racist paternalism is described as "Christian
concern, kindness, and sensitivity" as they were expressed in the early
days of colonialism and slavery. They were each based on the assumption
that the national minority "savages," being "uncivilized, lacking
culture, prone to violence, incapable of self-government, and motivated
more by biological, animal instinct, than human reasoning" -- are in
need of brute force to be kept in their place, or kindness, sensitivity
and uplifting. These poles of racist ideology have been revised as
history has progressed, but the revised expressions are variations on
the same theme. Overt racism expresses itself in our movement in white
comrades who demonstrate open, unveiled white chauvinism in relation to
national minorities. They openly attach racist stereotypes to national
minorities and treat them according to these views. [However, racist
paternalism [is the main form that white chauvinism takes in the
communist movement]{.mark}. This racist paternalism is largely
[influenced by]{.mark} the [bourgeois liberalism]{.mark} that developed
in this country.]{.underline} (See "Racism in the PWOC" p.5)

The ideology of white chauvinism permeates the Communist movement like
all other sectors of U.S. society. [[Communists have no]{.mark}
protective wall to [escape]{.mark} the influence of white chauvinism.
With the advent of the movements of the 60's, [the New Left adopted the
bourgeois liberalism that directed their]{.mark} paternalistic
[activities]{.mark} and views of national minorities. The communist
movement has this heritage and bourgeois liberalism is alive and well
among white Marxist-Leninists.]{.underline}

Bourgeois liberalism is primarily expressed in the communist movement by
[[white comrades]{.mark} [think]{.mark}ing that the way [to be
antiracist is to be a]{.mark} "[friend]{.mark} of the colored people."
They get involved in a mostly Latino rank-and-file caucus at work and
fight racism vigorously with the rest of the caucus members. But [they
don't struggle]{.mark} with white workers at the workplace [for the
same]{.mark} anti-racist [stand]{.mark}.]{.underline} They learn
Spanish, the history of Latin America, and Hispanic culture. They show
the Latino workers that they are on the side of "colored people" and can
really "relate" to them. Some may even marry or develop an intimate
relationship with a Latino to show the depth of their "anti-racism."
Rather than struggle with white workers to take up the demand for
equality on the shop floor and against the white workers' white
chauvinism, they run and tell the caucus members how racist the white
workers are and how they make fun of the way Latinos talk. The white
comrades of course show that they don't hold the same views of Latinos
because they understand Spanish and enjoy all aspects of Hispanic
culture. They actively disassociate themselves from those racist white
workers and show that they are true "friends" of the colored people.

[The underlying [assumption behind the "friend]{.mark} of the colored
people" [approach to the struggle]{.mark} against racism [is that
national minorities are irresponsible, lazy,]{.mark} prom to [violence,
and stupid]{.mark} not because they are inherently that way, but
[because they haven't been given the opportunity]{.mark} to be uplifted
by benevolent white people such as the comrade in the mostly Latino
rank-and-file caucus. What the national [minorities]{.mark}
[need]{.mark} is [a white "friend]{.mark}," who can help them fight for
educational opportunities, jobs, and better living
conditions.]{.underline}

The "friend of the colored people" views racism as "colored folks'
problem" and views him/herself as the benevolent white missionary
reaching out to lend a helping hand to the downtrodden and oppressed.
[This [perspective negates the fact that racism is the central
contradiction dividing the working class]{.mark} and that it is in the
interest of white workers to struggle against it. The ["friend]{.mark}
of the colored people" thereby [liquidates the struggle against racism
by negating the leading role white people have]{.mark} a responsibility
to play in the struggle against racism.]{.underline} White comrades who
adopt this view don't see the importance of taking up a struggle against
white chauvinism among white workers and winning them to the struggle
against racism. Instead, they see themselves as playing the role of the
helper, uplifting the downtrodden.

The bourgeois liberal, "friend of the colored people", views racism
among white workers the real obstacle to equality. The view is that if
it weren't for the "backward" white workers who oppose bussing, national
minorities could get a quality education; if it weren't for the
"backward" white workers who oppose affirmative action, national
minorities could get jobs. The "friend of the colored people" approach
says that white workers are incapable of struggling against the ideology
of white chauvinism, incapable of understanding that the struggle
against racism is in their own interest. That's why the white comrade in
the mostly Latino caucus does not struggle with white workers to join in
the fight for equality at the workplace. He/she views the white workers
as incapable of taking up the struggle against racism in the first place
and mainly responsible for the discriminatory practices on the job in
the second place. The bourgeois liberal sees white workers as hopelessly
racist, and views him/ herself as thoroughly antiracist--his/her
"friendship" with national minority people is proof of that.

[Thus [bourgeois liberalism]{.mark} is clearly [bound up with
petty-bourgeois chauvinism]{.mark}. [Comrades maintain a racist
paternalistic view]{.mark} [of]{.mark} and relationship with national
[minorities]{.mark}, and hold a profound disdain for white workers.
[Petty-bourgeois white comrades]{.mark} in our movement [think
that]{.mark} by virtue of their "[education" and liberal
"enlightenment]{.mark}" that [they "understand"]{.mark} the problems of
national [minorities]{.mark}. Their view of white workers, however, is
that they are totally incapable of comprehending anything but the

against racism. These comrades in fact maintain the same view of white

[Our [movement is]{.mark} mostly [white]{.mark} and
[petty-bourgeois]{.mark} in composition. It is [important]{.mark} for us
[to]{.mark} [understand and struggle]{.mark} against the
[ideology]{.mark} that influences our practice in keeping national

Petty-bourgeois chauvinism leads comrades to believe that those
currently in the movement, mostly white and petty-bourgeois comrades,

This is the view of the petty-bourgeoisie.

[The struggle against white chauvinism in the communist movement is

[When white comrades are unable and unwilling to wage an ideological

[Our movement has a "[white only]{.mark}" [policy]{.mark} for new people

about racism and the national question--not how the national minority

While our comrades maintain a white chauvinist view of national
minorities, national minorities represent the bulk of advanced workers
in the class. [[Our comrades' white chauvinism says that national
minorities are incapable of taking up the all-sided tasks of

understanding of these tasks]{.underline}]{.mark}. The advanced
character of the contributions of national minorities to the class

revolutionary nature of the liberation movements of oppressed national
minorities and the class composition of the majority of the national
minority population. The struggles national minorities engage in are not
won without a fight. [The bourgeoisie responded to the Civil Rights

opportunism that emerge within the class struggle and liberation

to struggle against those forms at opportunism.]{.underline} This is the

[[White comrades must assume their responsibility]{.mark} for playing

to [look honestly at their racist practice]{.mark} in relation to

If white comrades abandon the struggle against racism in our movement

### AT: Root Cause

#### Colonization is the [basis]{.underline} of modern capitalism.

**Stenport and Belanger 17** (Anna Westerstahl, Professor and Chair of
the School of Modern Languages at Georgia Institute of Technology,
Noelle, Independent Researcher, "The Politics of Color in the Arctic
Landscape: Blackness at the Center of Frederic Edwin Church\'s Aurora

ARTMargins, Vol. 6, Iss. 2, pp. 21--22, June 01, 2017,
https://doi.org/10.1162/ARTM_a_00174, iliyan)

Aurora Borealis reflects the themes of exploration and imperial
expansion that are echoed in the narrative subject of the painting and
in its brilliant display of color. In his article on color in the 19th
century, Peter John Brownlee notes, "After 1800 . . . an already brisk
international trade expanded significantly with the introduction of
extensive networks of mining, manufacturing, and distribution.
[As]{.underline} [part of the]{.underline} vast technological, economic,
and cultural [transformations]{.underline} of the 19^th^ century, these
[developments]{.underline} helped to further [blur]{.underline} already
**[indistinct boundaries]{.underline}** between science and art."31
Color had been a staple of the global economy for some time. Due in part
to their ease of transport, pigments and dyes had proved lucrative
commodities.32 By the 19^th^ century, chemically produced pigments and
dyes were being developed and were beginning to replace some of the
older mineral- and plantbased substances. However, the latter colors
were still being produced and used. In fact, the mining, extraction, and
production of dyes and pigments continued to contribute to the bustling
global economy at the time. [Alongside]{.underline} **[colonial
monopolies]{.underline}** on pigments and dyes, precious
**[metals]{.underline}** (and guano) [were **extracted**]{.underline}
[from]{.underline} **[colonized territories]{.underline}**.33 In the
19th century, the [[acquisition of]{.underline}
[territories]{.underline}]{.mark} **[ripe]{.underline}**
[with]{.underline} **[natural resources]{.underline}**, especially
precious metals, minerals, dyes, and people, was
[**[driving]{.underline}** [the]{.underline} **[global
economy]{.underline}**]{.mark}. Aurora Borealis, too, is grounded in
such a global circulation and trade of color.

Indeed, Church's painting was itself subject to global circulation.
Aurora Borealis made an Atlantic crossing when it was exhibited in
London in 1865.34 That year, along with Aurora Borealis, some of
Church's Latin American landscapes were brought together for another
London exhibition.35 In exhibiting these paintings together outside
their American context, the Arctic and Latin America were even more
closely connected and transformed into imaginary landscapes. In Aurora
Borealis, the ship [United States]{.underline} can thus [stand in
for]{.underline} the **[countless vessels]{.underline}** [sponsored
by]{.underline} **[Western governments]{.underline}** and private
enterprises that had sought the Northwest Passage in order to expand and
control international commerce. Simon Gikandi has demonstrated that this
"**[[new]{.underline}]{.mark}**" **[[global]{.mark}
[economy]{.mark}]{.underline}** [[had]{.underline}
**[blackness]{.underline}** [at its center]{.underline}]{.mark}, [and
that [the]{.mark}]{.underline} **[[violence]{.underline}]{.mark}**
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} [**[ugliness]{.underline}** [of]{.underline}
**[enslavement]{.underline}** [shaped]{.underline}]{.mark} [theories of
**taste**]{.underline} [and]{.underline} **[beauty]{.underline}** [as
well as [the practice of]{.mark}]{.underline} [**[high
culture]{.underline}**]{.mark}: "the ideal of politeness. . . was also
intimately connected to the commercial activities and the rather
impolite business of moneymaking; [in an]{.underline} age of

[[and]{.underline} **[candle fuel]{.underline}**]{.mark}
[and]{.underline} **[[whale bones]{.underline}]{.mark}**
[for]{.underline} **[corsets]{.underline}** ---[[another

**[[metaphorical]{.underline}]{.mark}**
"**[[whiteness]{.underline}]{.mark}**" has **[[occluded these

**[[slavery]{.underline}]{.mark}** [[and]{.mark} the]{.underline}

"**[[conjoined]{.underline}]{.mark}** [[by a]{.underline} **[new global

[[of]{.underline}]{.mark} **[[black bodies]{.underline}]{.mark}** on the

condition of possibility for all those fine practices and objects that
made up high culture.

#### Colonialism is the root cause of capitalism 

**Sanchez et al. 25** \[Dr. Phoebe Zoe Maria U. Sanchez is a Professor
of Sociology and History at the University of the Philippines Cebu and
served as a member of the board of directors to the Philippine
Sociological Society, Regletto Aldrich D. Imbong is a Ph.D., M.A. and
B.A. in Philosophy from the University of San Carlos, Philippines, and
University of the Philippines Cebu with focus on political theory and
philosophy of education, Matthew Ming-tak Chew holds an M.A. and Ph.D.
in Sociology from Princeton University and is currently a professor of

candidate in Sociology from Hong Kong Baptist University, 2025, "The
Palgrave Handbook on Decoloniality in Asia, Chapter Three: Capitalism,
Colonialism and Race,"
<https://link-springer-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/book/10.1007/978-981-96-2336-5>,
accessed 07/15/25\]s.taghi

[However, [without simultaneous access to colonies]{.mark} Northwestern
European [states would not have been able to]{.mark} increasingly
[specialize in industrial production]{.mark}, which they traded for
land- and labor-intensive goods from other regions]{.underline}
(Pomeranz 264ff ). The limited amount of land in Western Europe was not
a problem, as a combination of genocide and epidemics in America, the
slave trade, and Asian demand for American silver meant that the region
had access to an almost endless amount of land. [The colonies also
provided [a]{.mark} solution to the rapid population growth associated
with industrialization]{.underline}, as they became a popular
destination for emigrants unable to find work in Europe---a privilege
not accessible to later efforts at industrialization in the Global East
and South. [The **[rise of capitalism toward global hegemony]{.mark}
therefore [would not have been possible without]{.mark} its affinity
toward [colonial expansion]{.mark}**.]{.underline} In the labor
intensive system centered on East Asia/China, household-based production
prevailed, the majority of producers were not separated from their means
of subsistence, and states carefully curtailed the power of would-be
capitalists (Arrighi 2007, 57, 321ff; Graeber 2011, 259ff ). In
[contrast, European [capitalism]{.mark} is a capital- and
resource-intensive system that [combines both wage labor and unfree
labor]{.mark}, within it producers were separated from the means of
production and [capitalists]{.mark} have [managed to impose]{.mark}
their particular [class interest onto political
authorities]{.mark}.]{.underline} The latter point is central here. It
does not imply that capitalists somehow control states, but that [states
require corporate profits for taxes to fund their expenditures and are
thus structurally dependent on the success of local
capitalists]{.underline}. [The strength of the capitalist system until
the nineteenth century was not primarily based on economic power, but on
military superiority]{.underline}, as illustrated by the Opium Wars
(Arrighi 2007, 265ff ). This military power derives from its
embeddedness in the capitalist system. The commercialization of war and
continuous arms races in the leading capitalist states of an era have
led to self-reproducing spirals: military expenditure increases the
prosperity of a particular political formation, which in turn increases
taxes and enables new military expenditure. The [expansion of this
system through colonization and trade provides the resources that in
turn enable even further expansion. **[Colonialism is]{.mark} thus
[structurally written into capitalism's DNA]{.mark}**, already [played a
central role in the emergence]{.mark} of capitalism [and was]{.mark} a
[necessary condition for its rise to global
dominance]{.mark}]{.underline} []{.mark}via the dismantling of the East
Asian tribute trade system. From this perspective, [the **[conquest of
the Americas, including]{.mark} the [genocide of Indigenous
groups]{.mark}**, the slave trade and the race for Africa, **[is not a
legacy of pre-capitalist irrationality]{.mark}, [but]{.mark}** [**a
central component**]{.mark} of the globalizing competition for wealth
and power. [Capitalism is driven by endless accumulation]{.mark} and
this expansionary pressure creates incentives for coalitions of states
with the companies located within them to seek unilateral access to
other territories, dispossessing land and labor]{.underline}, tilting
terms of trade in their favor via military force, etc. These systemic
pressures are important to keep in mind vis-à-vis current debates about
the geopolitical and economic implications of the rise of East Asia and
the possibility of a multi-polar world order. For a consideration of
capitalism and racism, such a global perspective on the central role of
colonialism for capitalism is one side of the coin; the other is the
associated formation of racist structures within political territories.
We turn to the latter in the next section.

#### More ev

**Blaut 89** \[J.M. Blaut was an American professor of anthropology and
geography at the University of Illinois at Chicago. His studies focused
on agricultural microgeography (geographical activity of villagers),
cultural ecology, theory of nationalism, philosophy of science,
historiography and the relations between the First and the Third World.
He was a critic of Eurocentrism, 1989, "Colonialism and the Rise of
Capitalism," published in the Science and Society Journal,
<https://www-jstor-org.proxy.lib.umich.edu/stable/40404472?sid=primo&seq=25>,
accessed 07/15/25\]s.taghi

In 1492, Europe was moving out of feudalism and toward capitalism, but
at a sluggish pace. The English woolen industry was growing, not (as is
often claimed) as a step toward imminent industrialization, but as a
result of the decline of woolens in other parts of Europe (Miskimin,
1969). Rural growth was taking place but this reflected in part a
population recovery after the great plagues and in part an ongoing and
not at all sudden commercialization (Abel, 1980). Towns were growing
only slowly, and urban population was still only a tiny fraction of
total population (de Vries, 1984). (Some extra-European regions had
larger fractions and larger cities.) In fact, there were strong
indications of economic downturn rather than growth (Hodgett, 1972;
Lopez and Miskimin, 1961-62; Smith, 1969). The Italian Renaissance was
neither an economic nor a technological revolution (Lopez, 1953;
Thorndyke, 1943), and did not raise the Italian protocapitalist centers
above the level of other centers in the hemispheric network. Nothing in
the European landscape of 1492 would suggest that a revolutionary
transformation was about to take place. [Just a few decades later the
rate of change speeded up dramatically, and Europe clearly entered a
period of rapid metamorphosis. Change continued to accelerate, and now
we witness the true rise of the bourgeoisie. Non-industrial [capitalist
production increased rapidly]{.mark} in northwestern Europe, while
feudal production in that region declined toward extinction.
Protocapitalist centers expanded]{.underline}. The bourgeoisie gained
power as a class and eventually pulled off its bourgeois revolution,
taking full power in the Low Countries and England in the mid-17^th^
century and coming near to that stage in a number of other countries (I
will use the date 1688, the date of England's "Glorius Revolution," as
the token for the bourgeois revolution, as I am using 1492 as the token
for the origin of colonialism.) Did this transformation result from
internal causes within Europe? [I will argue rather that external
factors - broadly, **[colonialism]{.mark} -- [initiated the
transformation]{.mark},** continued to feed it, [and sparked]{.mark}
those [internal changes which eventually produced]{.mark} the
[revolutionary transformations]{.mark} in economy and society but [which
could not have done so without]{.mark} the continuing impact of
[colonialism]{.mark} throughout the period of 1492-1688. In a sense,
then, **[colonialism led to the rise of
capitalism]{.mark}**.]{.underline} (Or look at it this way: if Indian
protocapitalist centers had been the ones to reach first, and then
conquer, America, and so initiate a period of Indian colonialism,
capitalism would have risen first in India. Or Songhay, China, etc.)
[**[Colonial enterprise was from the outset]{.mark} a matter [of capital
accumulation]{.mark}**.]{.underline} No matter if some elements of
feudalism were incorporated in legal and land-granting systems, and if
the Iberian governments took a substantial (though usually
over-estimated) share of the profits. No matter if the people involved
in the process, in the colonial world and in Europe, played a great
number of different roles, some of which defy classification as to mode
of production. Questions pertaining to these matters deserve discussion
among Marxists but they do not alter the empirical reality which we are
discussing here. [The [goal of all European individuals]{.mark} and
groups [involved in]{.mark} the [colonial process]{.mark} (clergy apart)
[was to make money]{.mark}.]{.underline} The leading group was the
protocapitalist class, not only merchants but also industrialists,
profit-minded landlords (titled and untitled), shipowners, carters,
shopkeepers, small-scale artisan-manufacturers, and others; not only
Iberian but also Italian, Flemish, Dutch, German, English, French. [This
class [community took]{.mark} its [profit from American]{.mark} (and
secondarily Asian and African) [enterprise and invested]{.mark} part of
[it in Europe]{.mark}, buying land and developing commercial
agriculture, [developing industries]{.mark} which were [associated
with]{.mark} the [expanding colonial enterprise or]{.mark} with the
[economic growth within]{.mark} Europe [which]{.mark} the [colonial
enterprise engendered]{.mark}.]{.underline} Part of the accumulated
capital was ploughed back into additional colonial risk enterprise, in
the Americas and in the new trading enterprises in southern Asia,
Africa, and the Levant. [[Colonial enterprise]{.mark} in the 16^th^
century and the early 17^th^ [generated]{.mark} an [immense]{.mark}
amount of [capital]{.mark}, and did so in many ways.]{.underline} One
was gold and silver mining (and plunder). A second was plantation
agriculture, principally, in that period, in Brazil. A third was the
trade with Asia in spices, cloth, and the like. A fourth and by no means
minor element was the profit returned to European houses from a great
variety of productive and commercial enterprises in the colonial world,
including profit on production for local use, on imports from Europe, on
a variety of secondary exports from the Americas (leather, dyestuffs,
etc.), on intercontinental trade outside of Europe (e.g., between Mexico
and Peru, Mexico and the Philippines, Africa and Asia), and the like. A
fifth way of accumulating was slaving. A sixth, piracy (see Dunn, 1972).
And there were others. Notice that most of this is normal capital
accumulation, not really that slippery thing called "primitive
accumulation." Surplus value from wage labor, not to mention forced
labor, was involved in all of this, and most of it was value from
production, not simply from trade. [[Nothing could be more erroneous
than to]{.mark} dismiss 16^th^-century colonial enterprise as a compound
of primitive accumulation and exchange (falsely equating transportation
with exchange); then to contrast this was supposedly genuine production
in Europe; and then to [declare]{.mark} it thus proven that [colonial
enterprise]{.mark} in that century [had little to do with the rise of
capitalism]{.mark} in Europe because only *production* can change the
mode of production. [Colonialism involved massive production]{.mark},
massive [exchange]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} massive [capital
accumula]{.mark}tion.]{.underline}

## Antiblackness K

### AT: Afropessimism 

#### Afropessimists characterization of settler colonialism is inaccurate and unhelpful -- it forecloses relationality for resisting domination 

**Day '15** -- Ikyo Day is an associate professor of English at Mount

### Perm -- Dialectics 

#### This debate is not a question of which structure caused the other, blackness and colonialism exist dialectical relation co-producing modern violence 

**Day '15** -- Ikyo Day is an associate professor of English at Mount

[a]{.underline} **[larger]{.underline}** [question]{.underline}

**[Afro]{.mark}-[pessimism]{.mark}**[, I want to

[**[erasure]{.mark}** [of]{.mark} [Indigenous]{.mark}

[[from]{.underline}]{.mark} the **[[ground]{.underline}]{.mark}**
**[it]{.underline}** doesn't stand on, or [is removed from]{.underline}.
For the same reason that the economic reductionism of orthodox Marxism
has been discredited, such [[an **argument** that
**frames**]{.underline}]{.mark} [racial]{.underline}
[[slavery]{.underline} [as]{.underline} [a **base**

[[race]{.underline}]{.mark} [[and]{.mark} **[colonialism]{.mark}**

**[settler colonial]{.mark}** [racial]{.mark}
**[state]{.mark}**.]{.underline}

Putting colonial land and enslaved labor at the center of a dialectical
analysis, we can see that blackness is neither reducible to Indigenous
land nor Indigeneity to enslaved labor. Indigenous peoples and slaves
are not reducible to each other because settler colonialism abides by a
dual logic that is originally driven to eliminate Native peoples from
land and mix the land with enslaved black labor. If land is the basis of
settler colonialists' relationship to Indigenous peoples, it is labor
that frames that relationship with enslaved peoples. We can draw on
Patrick Wolfe's important points about the heterogeneous racial effects
of such a settler formation based on Indigenous land and enslaved labor.
To summarize those points, the racial content of Indigenous peoples is
the mirror opposite of blackness. From the beginning, an eliminatory
project was driven to reduce Native populations through genocidal wars
and later through statistical elimination through blood quantum and
assimilationist policies. For slaves, an opposite logic of exclusion was
driven to increase, not eliminate, the population of slaves. [[One
**logic** does not **cause** the other]{.underline}]{.mark};
[rather]{.underline}, [[they **work together**]{.underline}
[to]{.underline} **[serve]{.underline}**]{.mark} a unitary end in
[increasing **[white settler property]{.mark}**]{.underline}
[in]{.underline} the form of **[land]{.underline}** [and]{.underline} an
[**enslaved labor** force]{.underline}. [As a result]{.underline},
[in]{.underline} [the]{.underline} **[postemancipation]{.underline}**,
postfrontier [era]{.underline}, [the racial content of Indigenous
peoples is entirely]{.underline} dissolvable and
**[eradicable]{.underline}**. [Alternatively]{.underline}, the [racial
content of **blackness** remains **absolute**]{.underline} and
essential, and [maintains an **infinite** capacity to
**contaminate**]{.underline}. As Wolfe states, "the [respective
racializations]{.underline} . . . [were **diametrically**]{.underline}
**[opposed]{.underline}**, in a manner that reflected and preserved the
foundational distinction between land and labor. [For]{.underline}
[whereas]{.underline} [race for black people became an **indelible
trait**]{.underline} that would survive any amount of admixture,
[race]{.underline} for ~~Indians~~ [became an inherently **descending**
quantity]{.underline} [that]{.underline} [was]{.underline} terminally
[susceptible to **dilution**]{.underline}."59 One consequence is that
the phrase "separate but equal" can take two meanings: as either an
injurious legal relic or a sovereign politics of the future.60 Given
this stark distinction in racial ontologies, [[any]{.underline}]{.mark}
critical [[theory]{.underline}]{.mark} [[that]{.mark} [views]{.mark}
**[race]{.mark}** [and]{.mark} **[colonialism]{.mark}**]{.underline}
[[as]{.underline}]{.mark} a [**[causal]{.underline}**
[rather]{.underline} [than]{.underline}

[**[incapable]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[exposing]{.underline}]{.mark} these **[[inextricable]{.mark}
[logics]{.mark}]{.underline}** of settler colonialism.

### Perm -- King

#### The critique pits Black and Indigenous studies against each other- the perm does presume any specific form of relationality but bands together to resist structures of domination and oppression 

**King et al. '20** -- Tiffany Lethabo King. Jenell Navarro. Andrea
Smith. (May 2020, 'Beyond Incommensurability: Toward an Otherwise Stance
On Black and Indigenous Relationality', Duke University Press,
https://doi-org.proxy.lib.umich.edu/10.1215/9781478012023-001, soyam)

[The relationship]{.underline} [between]{.underline} [**Native**
**genocide**]{.underline} [and]{.underline}
**[anti-Blackness]{.underline}** [has been **articulated**]{.underline}
sometimes [in terms]{.underline} [of presumed
**solidarity**]{.underline} or comparison, such as the notion that
[Native peoples **harbored** runaway **slaves**]{.underline} and that
[Black fugitives **assisted** Native peoples in **armed**]{.underline}
**[struggle]{.underline}** [against]{.underline} settler
[encroachment]{.underline}. [Sometimes]{.underline} it has been
[articulated]{.underline} [in terms of **antagonism**]{.underline}, such
as the focus on [Native peoples who]{.underline} [**owned**
slaves]{.underline} and on [enslaved]{.underline} or indentured [Black
people who participated]{.underline} in settler [raids]{.underline} on
Native nations. Nowadays, [it seems to be in terms of
incommensurability,]{.underline} which asserts a lack of
commonality/relationality between Black and Native folks. This project
emerges from us thinking that **[all of these modes are
insufficient]{.underline}**. While certainly solidarity, antagonism, and
incommensurability are distinct and no one mode of relationality can be
presumed, at the same time [it is **illogical** to presume we can talk
about **any**]{.underline} mode [without]{.underline} doing it [in
**relationship** with **one** **another**]{.underline}. If we submit
momentarily to the popular position that Black and Native peoples and,
by extension, [Black and Native]{.underline} politics are at an impasse
represented by their incommensurability, then the flip side of being
stuck together---or [this]{.underline}
**[stuckness]{.underline}**---[is]{.underline} already [a form of
**relationality**]{.underline}. As a result, we think Édouard Glissant's
"relation" seems to be a helpful starting point: Relation . . . does not
act upon prime elements that are separable or reducible. If this were
true, it would itself be reduced to some mechanics capable of being
taken apart or reproduced. It does not precede itself in its action and
presupposes no a priori. It is the boundless effort of the world, to
become realized in its totality, that is to evade rest. One does not
first enter Relation, as one might enter a religion. One does not first
conceive of it the way we have expected to conceive of Being. It seems
that [much **analysis** of the **relationship** between]{.underline}
Indigenous genocide/[**settler** **colonialism**]{.underline}
[and]{.underline} [**anti**-**Blackness**]{.underline}
[tends]{.underline} [to be **prescriptive**]{.underline} [in that the
analysis]{.underline} [presumes]{.underline} a certain [prescribed
politic]{.underline}---whether it be a call for solidarity in a certain
way or a call to reject solidarity. But [**analysis** of]{.underline}
[relationality]{.underline} [suggests]{.underline} something
otherwise---that the **[relationality]{.underline}**
[between]{.underline} [genocide]{.underline} [and]{.underline}
**[anti]{.underline}**-**[Blackness]{.underline}** [is not]{.underline}
fixed and [easily **knowable**]{.underline}. In addition, to borrow from
Antonio Viego's Dead Subjects, there is an [imperative in the
academy]{.underline} [to make]{.underline} [Native peoples
knowable]{.underline} and [to]{.underline} [presume]{.underline} that
[**Black peoples** are **already** **known**]{.underline}.2 Thus, not
surprisingly, the relationship between the two is presumed to be fully
representable and it keeps Black and Indigenous communities in isolation
from one another, which is a settler desire/ dream. Thus, we would
prefer an approach that [does not]{.underline} [presume]{.underline}
[an]{.underline} "[answer]{.underline}" but [instead]{.underline}
[seeks]{.underline} [to]{.underline} [ask]{.underline}
[questions]{.underline} about the [complexities]{.underline} [of this
relation]{.underline}, and [hence]{.underline} the political
[possibilities]{.underline} that emerge from asking these questions and
engaging in the process of relation. [This desire not to
presume]{.underline} an answer [mediates any attempt to trace a
genealogy to this conversation with the respective fields of Native
studies and Black studies]{.underline} because to tell a story of either
field tends to prescribe the outcome of any conversation between the
fields. For instance, we could trace the development of Native studies
through the influential work of Elizabeth Cook-Lynn's "Who Stole Native
Studies" and Winona Stevenson's "'Ethnic' Assimilates 'Indigenous.'"3
These works argued that Native studies should distance itself from
ethnic studies (and presumably from Black studies by extension) because
otherwise the field of ethnic studies would relegate Native peoples to a
racial minority status rather than as peoples seeking decolonization.
Cook-Lynn and Wheeler contended that engaging Native studies with ethnic
studies, postcolonial studies, and so on, which do not share a concern
for the liberation of Native peoples, could have the effect of
domesticating Native studies into a multiculturalist project of
representation within the academy instead of one that defended Native
nations' claims to sovereignty. While there is much to be gained by
tracing the genealogy of Native studies through these works, how does
this genealogy presume that Black studies (or ethnic studies generally
speaking) is fundamentally about articulating a racial minority status?
Or we could center the work of Lee Maracle and Roberto Mendoza who
argued that Native people and Native studies needed to be in
conversation with radical political thought emerging from all sectors of
society.4 Such a genealogy would more easily enable a conversation
between Native studies and other fields of thought. At the same time,
their work often presumed a more Marxist framework as the point of
conversation between Native studies and other fields. Thus, we wonder
what might be enabled differently from a conversation that began from
dif­ferent theoretical assumptions? The point here is not to disparage
the importance of these works. Rather, it is to say that to delimit the
fields of Native studies and Black studies is to delimit the
possibilities of conversations between the two. And to have this
conversation is to simultaneously open up what the fields of Native
studies and Black studies can be. Similarly, mapping genealogies of
Black studies might also work to stifle emergent, lesser known, and
otherwise conversations between the two fields. For one, the task of
periodizing the field and charting its geographic coordinates is already
a contested project. Further, attending to how multiple forms and
practices of Black study have turned their scholarly attention to
Indigenous peoples might privilege some forms of knowledge production
and their political projects over others. The aforementioned project
requires a project of its own. For example, Arika Easley-Houser examines
antebellum African American print culture (newspapers, political
speeches, David Walker's Appeal) in order to track the ways African
American writers thought and talked about Native Americans in the
nineteenth century.5 Easley-Houser argues that several dif­ferent
political projects, ranging from ones that sought alliances with Native
peoples to comparative projects that tried to prove African American
superiority to Native peoples and even investigations of Native
practices of enslavement, motivated Black nineteenthcentury writing on
Native peoples.6 Shortly after founding the Association for the Study of
African American Life and History in 1915, Carter G. Woodson authored
the article "The Relations of Negroes and Indians in Massachusetts" in
1920 in the Journal of Negro History. Throughout the twentieth century
there were intermittent attempts on the part of Black individuals and
institutions to document and study Black and Native histories and
exchanges in America. During the late 1960s and early 1970s, militant
anti-imperialist and anticapitalist activism animated by the Black Power
movement in the US and Third World internationalism birthed the first
Black studies programs. The strong ties between Black activism and the
development of academic departments created fertile ground for
conversations between Black and Native scholars and activists.
[Revisiting Black, Native, and ethnic studies' radical roots encourages
a commemoration of histories of solidarity and shared struggle while it
also exposes the pressure that universities put on fields like Black
studies to "define" itself and distinguish itself from other ethnic
studies programs like Native studies]{.underline}. In 1974, Robert Allen
wrote about the ways that [Black studies and ethnic studies were
b]{.underline}eing "[counter-posed" and forced into an "antagonistic
relationship" with one another in the academy.]{.underline}7 While this
genealogical approach thus far has limited itself to Black studies in
the US, [recounting this history helps expose the ways that the settler
colonial university worked (and continues) to pit fields like Black and
Native studies against one another and prevent generative
dialogue]{.underline}.8
